Salah — Prayer
intermediate
6 min
Speech Invalidates Prayer Even if Forgetful or Mistaken
Explaining that speech invalidates prayer and defending against the objection of forgetfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that speech invalidates prayer even if forgetful or mistaken, identify the primary hadith proof, and explain why the hadith of lifting error does not negate invalidity.

Core Idea
Any human speech in prayer invalidates it — even a single forgetful word — because the Prophet explicitly forbade all speech.

This passage from Nur al-Idah and its sharh addresses one of the most fundamental rules of salah: the prohibition of speech. The author asserts that speech invalidates prayer without exception, even if uttered forgetfully or by mistake. This ruling is grounded in a sahih hadith that limits prayer to dhikr, takbir, and Quran. The lesson will walk through the proof and rebut a common objection based on the hadith of lifted accountability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is speech (الكلمة). The ruling is that it invalidates prayer (يفسد الصلاة). The hukm level is batil (the prayer becomes void).
Invalidation here means the prayer must be repeated, not just compensated with sujud al-sahw.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith of Mu'awiyah ibn al-Hakam al-Sulami: 'Indeed this prayer does not contain anything of human speech; it is only tasbih, takbir, and recitation of the Quran.' This is a sahih hadith (Muslim).
Also narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tabarani with similar wording.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'لا يَصْلُحُ فِيهَا شَيْءٌ مِنْ كَلَامِ النَّاسِ' uses 'shay' (an indefinite noun) in the context of negation. According to Hanafi usul, this gives generality: all human speech is negated from being suitable in prayer. Therefore, any speech invalidates.
The indefinite in negation (النكرة في سياق النفي) indicates عموم (generality).
4
Address the counter-dalil
The hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان' might be raised to argue that forgetful speech is excused. The Hanafi response: this hadith refers to the final ruling in the hereafter (sin and punishment), not the worldly ruling of invalidity. The worldly ruling is based on the existence of the cause (speech), which is present.
The commentator explains that the hadith is مشترك (shared between two meanings) and has no generality; it applies only to the afterlife.
5
Note the cross-madhhab disagreement
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that forgetful speech does not invalidate prayer based on the same lifting hadith. The practical consequence (ثمرة الخلاف): in Hanafi, the prayer is invalid and must be repeated; in Shafi'i, it remains valid.
This disagreement stems from different usul principles regarding the scope of the lifting hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يفسد الصلاة
وهو ثمانية وستون شيئاً: الكلمة، ولو سهواً، أو خطأ.
باب ما يفسد الصلاة
من العوارض المكتسبة والسماوية، والفساد والبطلان في العبادات سيان، وفي المعاملات
(1)
فاسد
كالبيع مفترقان، وفي « القاموس » : فسد كنصر وعقد وكرم فساداً أو فسوداً، ضد صلح فهو وفسيد من فسد ولم يسمع ا انفسد. انتهى. وحصر المفسد بالعد تقريباً لا تحديداً فقال: (وهو ثمانية وستون شيئاً ) . منه : ( الكلمة) تفسد الصلاة وإن لم تكن مفيدة كـ يا »، (وَلَو) نطق بها (سهواً) يظن كونه ليس في الصلاة، (أو) نطق بها (خَطَأ) كما لو أراد أن يقول : يا أيها الناس، فقال: يا زيد، ولو جهل كونه مفسداً، كما لو تعمده، لقوله : ( إِنَّ هَذِهِ الصَّلاةَ لا يَصْلُحُ فِيهَا شَيْءٌ مِنْ كَلَامِ النَّاسِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ ) وفي رواية: ( إِنَّمَا هِيَ التَّسْبِيحُ وَالتَّكْبِيرُ وَقِرَاءةُ الْقُرْآنِ ) وفي لفظ للطبراني في ( معجمه » : « إِنَّ صَلاتَنَا لا يَحِلُّ فيها شيءٌ مِنْ كلامِ النَّاسِ » (۳) وما لا يحل ولا يصلح في فمباشرته تفسدها، لقوله : ( الكلام ينقصُ الصَّلاةَ ولا ينقض الوضوء ) وشيء، نكرة في موضع النفي فتعم، فينتفي الصلاح من جميع الوجوه فتبطل به الصلاة ولو تكلم نائماً على المختار، وأما قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «رفعَ عن أمَّتِي الخَطَأ والنِّسْيانُ وما استكرهوا عليه». فالمراد به: رفـــع الحكم؛ لأن الخطأ والنسيان والإكراه ليس بمرفوع لحصول ذلك مشاهدة.
الصلاة
وحكمه نوعان: دنيوي وأخروي فالدنيوي الجواز أو الفساد ومبناهمــا علـى وجـود السبب. والثاني الأخروي: الثواب أو العقاب، ومبناهما على وجود العزيمة. فصار الحديث مشتركاً وهـو لا عموم له، وقد أُريد حكم الآخرة فانتفى الآخر، ولا يصح أن يقال على السلام: سهواً، لأنه دعاء مـــن وجه فباعتباره لا تبطل إذا سلَّم ناسياً، وكلام من وجه، فباعتباره تبطل إذا تعمده في غير محله عملاً بالشبهتين، وإنما عُفِي القليل من العمل؛ لأن أصله لا يمكن الاحتراز عنه، ه، لأن في الحي حركات ليست من الصلاة طبعاً، فعفي ما لم يكثر ويدخل في حدّ ما يمكن الاحتراز عنه، ولهذا يستوي فيه العمد والنسيان، وليس الكلام كذلك، لأنه ليس من طبعه أن يتكلم، فلا يعفى، ولا يجوز قياسه على الصوم
Chapter on what invalidates prayer. It is sixty-eight things: speech, even if forgetfully or mistakenly. Chapter on what invalidates prayer from acquired and heavenly impediments. Invalidity and nullification in acts of worship are the same, while in transactions like sale they are distinct. In al-Qamus: 'fasada' like nasara and 'aqada and karuma, meaning corruption, opposite of righteousness; it is fasid and fasid from fasada, and lam yusma' 'infasada.' End. Restricting the invalidators by number is approximate, not definitive. So he said: (It is sixty-eight things). Among them: (speech) invalidates prayer even if not beneficial like 'Ya', (even if) uttered (forgetfully) thinking one is not in prayer, (or) uttered (mistakenly) as if he wanted to say 'O people' but said 'O Zayd' even if ignorant of it being invalidating, just as if intentional; due to his statement: 'Indeed this prayer does not contain anything of human speech; rather it is' and in a narration: 'Rather it is glorification, takbir, and recitation of the Quran.' And in a wording of al-Tabarani in his Mu'jam: 'Indeed our prayer does not permit anything of human speech.' And what is not permitted and not suitable, engaging in it invalidates it; due to his statement: 'Speech diminishes prayer and does not break wudu.' 'Shay' (thing) is an indefinite in the context of negation, thus it generalizes; so suitability is negated from all aspects, thus prayer becomes invalid by it, even if one speaks while sleeping on the preferred view. As for his statement, peace be upon him: 'Lifted from my nation error, forgetfulness, and what they are forced upon' – the intent is lifting of the [final] ruling; because error, forgetfulness, and coercion are not non-existent, as they are observed. Prayer. Its ruling is two types: worldly and otherworldly. The worldly is permissibility or invalidity, and they are based on the existence of the cause. The second otherworldly is reward or punishment, and they are based on the presence of resolve. So the hadith becomes shared [between two meanings] and it has no generality; and the otherworldly ruling was intended [in the hadith], so the worldly [ruling] is not negated. And it is not correct to say [the ruling] on salam: forgetfully, because it is supplication from one aspect, so from that aspect it does not invalidate if one says salam forgetfully; and human speech from another aspect, so from that aspect it invalidates if done intentionally in its improper place, acting on both considerations. Indeed, slight action is excused because its origin cannot be avoided, because in a living being there are movements not from prayer by nature, so it is excused as long as it is not excessive and enters the limit of what can be avoided; for this reason intention and forgetfulness are equal in it [action]. Speech is not like that, because it is not natural for one to speak, so it is not excused. And it is not permissible to analogize it to fasting.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a sharh expanding the matn on the chapter of invalidators of prayer. It begins by stating that speech (الكلمة) invalidates prayer, whether it is beneficial or not, and whether uttered forgetfully (ظاناً أنه ليس في الصلاة) or mistakenly (as when one intends to say 'O people' but says 'O Zayd'). Ignorance of the ruling is treated like intention. The proof is the authentic hadith: 'This prayer does not suit anything of human speech; it is only tasbih, takbir, and Quran recitation.' The hadith negates suitability for all speech, using the indefinite 'shay' in the context of negation, which gives generality. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: the hadith 'Lifted from my nation error, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced upon' – he explains that this lifting applies to the otherworldly ruling (sin), not the worldly ruling (invalidity). Thus forgetful speech still invalidates. He further distinguishes speech from slight actions (al-'amal al-qalil): actions are excused because they are unavoidable; speech is not excused because it is not natural to prayer. Therefore, speech cannot be analogized to fasting (where forgetful eating does not invalidate according to Hanafis). The discussion also touches on the salam: when one says salam forgetfully, it is considered from two aspects – as supplication (dua) and as human speech; if forgetful, it does not invalidate based on the dua aspect.

Key Terms
الكلمة (al-kalimah): Any utterance, whether meaningful or not, that is not dhikr or Quran. Even a single word like 'Ya' invalidates prayer.
سهواً (sahwan): Forgetfully, when the speaker is unaware they are in prayer, thinking they have finished or are not in salah.
خطأ (khata'an): Mistakenly, when one intends to say something permissible but mistakenly utters something unintended, like saying 'Ya Zayd' instead of 'Ya ayyuha al-nas'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, does speaking a single word forgetfully invalidate the prayer?
A) No, because it was forgetful and the hadith lifts error.
B) Yes, it invalidates because the hadith prohibits all human speech in prayer.
C) It depends on whether the word is beneficial or not.
D) It invalidates only if the worshiper intended to speak.

Key Takeaway
Any human speech in prayer invalidates it, even if forgetful or mistaken, because the Prophet's hadith is general and the hadith of lifted error does not apply to worldly rulings.

Salah — Prayer

Invalidators of Prayer: Speech, Greeting, and Excessive Action
List of invalidators with proof and cross-madhhab comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which actions invalidate prayer according to the Hanafi school and apply the criterion for distinguishing invalidating dua from permissible dua.

Core Idea
Any action that breaks the distinct form of prayer invalidates it, even if forgetful.

This passage from Sharh Nur al-Idah lists several actions that invalidate prayer: dua that resembles human speech, greeting with the intention of tahiyyah, responding to greeting, and excessive action. It also clarifies exceptions like unavoidable speech from sneezing and permissible supplications from the Quran or Sunnah. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for maintaining the validity of salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalidator
Read the text and list each action explicitly mentioned as invalidating (mufsid). These include certain duas, greeting, responding, and excessive action.
The text also mentions what does NOT invalidate, such as sneezing/coughing that produces speech.
2
Apply the criterion for dua
For dua, distinguish between requests that could be made to a human (invalidating) and those that cannot (not invalidating). The guideline from al-Marghinani is key.
If the dua is from Quran, Sunnah, or conventional supplications (e.g., asking for Paradise), it does not invalidate.
3
Analyze greeting and response
Note that greeting (saying salaam) with the intention of greeting invalidates, even if forgetfully. Similarly, responding by tongue or handshake invalidates because it constitutes speech in meaning.
The ruling applies even if the response is forgetful.
4
Define excessive action
Recognize that the text states 'excessive action' invalidates but 'little action' does not. The Hanafi school defines excessive action functionally: what makes an observer think the person is not praying.
This is a relative standard, not a fixed number of movements.
5
Compare with Shafi'i view
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i does not invalidate prayer by dua that resembles human speech. Understand the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) in rulings.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi in our school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لصوم، الدعاء بما يُشْبِهُ كلامنا، والسَّلامُ بِنيَّهِ التَّحِيَّةِ، ولو ساهياً. وردُّ السّلام بلسانه، أو بالمصافحة، والعمل الكثير. لأن حالة الصلاة مذكرة لكونها على هيئة مخصوصة تخالف العادة في زمن يسير، فلا يكثر لنسيان فيها بخلاف الصوم وفي ( المحيط ) : لو عطس أو تجشأ فحصل منـه كـلام لا تفسد، لتعــذر لاحتراز عنه (وَ) يفسدها (الدّعاءُ بما يُشبِهُ كلامنا نحو قوله: اللهم ألبسني ثوب كذا، أو زوجني مرأة، وأفرده بالذكر وإن حصل دخل بالتكلم؛ لأن الإمام الشافعي رحمه الله لا يفسدها بالدعــاء ذلك، وذكر في ( البحر ) عن المرغيناني ضابطاً فقال: الحاصل أنه إذا دعا بما جاء في الصلاة، أو القرآن، أو في المأثور، لا تفسد صلاته، وإن لم يكن في القرآن، أو المأثور، ولا يستحيل سؤاله ن العباد تفسد. انتهى. كقوله: اللهم أطعمني، أو اقض ديني أو ارزقني فلانة، على الصحيح، ومـا تحال طلبه من العباد فليس من كلامنا، مثل طلب العافية والمغفرة والرزق كقوله: اللهم ارزقني من بقلها، وقثائها، وفومها، وعدسها، وبصلها لا تفسد صلاته، ولو قال: اللهم ارزقني فوماً وبقلاً وقثاءً عدساً وبصلاً تفسد لما قلنا. (وَ) يفسدها ( السَّلامُ بنيَّهِ التَّحِيَّةِ) وإن لم يقل: عليكـم (وَلـو) كـان سَاهِيَاً)، كما لو تعمده في غير محله لا بقصد التحلل ساهياً ) لما قدمناه. (و) يفسدها (رد :سلام بلسانه ولو سهواً؛ لأنه من كلام الناس، (أو) رد السلام بِالمُصَافَحَةِ) (۳) لأنه كلام معنى و) يفسدها (العملُ الكثيرُ ) لا القليل
For fasting? Dua that resembles our speech, and greeting with the intention of tahiyyah, even if forgetfully. And responding to greeting with the tongue, or by handshake, and excessive action. Because the state of prayer is reminding? being on a specific form that contradicts custom in a short time, so forgetfulness does not increase in it unlike fasting. In al-Muhit: if he sneezes or belches and speech occurs from him, it does not invalidate, due to impossibility of guarding against it. And it invalidates it: dua that resembles our speech like saying: O Allah, clothe me such garment, or marry me a woman, and he singled it out by mention, even if he included it? because Imam al-Shafi'i does not invalidate by such dua. And in al-Bahr from al-Marghinani a rule: the summary is that if he prays with what is in prayer or Quran or transmitted tradition, his prayer is not invalidated; if it is not in Quran or transmitted tradition and it is not impossible to ask from servants, it invalidates. End. Like saying: O Allah, feed me, or pay my debt, or provide me with so-and-so, on the correct view. And what is possible to ask from servants is not from our speech, like asking for well-being, forgiveness, and provision; like saying: O Allah, provide me from its greens, its cucumbers, its garlic, its lentils, its onions, does not invalidate his prayer. And if he says: O Allah, provide me garlic and greens and cucumber lentils and onions, it invalidates due to what we said. And it invalidates it: greeting with intention of tahiyyah even if he did not say 'alaykum, even if forgetful, like if he intended it in other than its place not intending tahlil forgetfully, due to what we presented. And it invalidates it: responding to greeting with his tongue even if forgetfully, because it is from people's speech, or responding to greeting by handshake, because it is speech in meaning. And it invalidates it: excessive action, not little.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation about what invalidates prayer. The author lists several invalidators: First, dua that resembles human speech (i.e., requesting worldly matters normally asked of people) invalidates prayer, even if done forgetfully. However, if the dua is from the Quran, prophetic traditions, or conventional supplications (like asking for Paradise), it does not invalidate. The key distinction is whether the request could be made to a human being. If it could (like asking for a garment or marriage), it invalidates; if it could not (like asking for forgiveness or provision in a general sense), it does not. The same applies to greeting (saying salaam) with the intention of tahiyyah (greeting) and responding to greeting, whether by tongue or handshake. Excessive action ('amal kathir) also invalidates, while little action does not. The ruling is based on the principle that prayer has a specific form that distinguishes it from ordinary acts, so excessive motion breaks that form. The author notes that sneezing or belching that produces speech does not invalidate because it is unavoidable. The Hanafi position is that these actions invalidate prayer, unlike Imam al-Shafi'i who does not invalidate by such dua. The relied-upon view is that the criterion for excessive action is whether an observer would think the person is not in prayer.

Key Terms
العمل الكثير (al-'amal al-kathir): Excessive action that invalidates prayer; defined by the author later in the text based on five opinions.
الدعاء بما يشبه كلامنا (al-du'a bima yushbihu kalamina): Dua that resembles human speech in that it asks for something normally requested from people.
التسليم بنية التحية (al-taslim bi niyyat al-tahiyyah): Saying salaam with the intention of greeting someone (not as part of prayer).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following actions would invalidate prayer even if done forgetfully?
A) Sneezing and saying 'Alhamdulillah'
B) Intentionally greeting someone with salaam
C) Reciting a verse from the Quran
D) Wiping one's nose once

Key Takeaway
The invalidators of prayer hinge on whether the action disrupts the distinct form of salah; dua and greeting are judged by their content and intention, while excessive action is judged by observer perception.

Five Opinions on Excessive Action in Prayer
Five Hanafi opinions defining excessive action in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five Hanafi definitions of excessive action ('amal kathir) in prayer and identify the preferred opinion with its reasoning.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school flexibly defines excessive prayer action; the preferred view defers to the worshipper's own judgment.

The primary texts of prayer do not give a quantitative measure for excessive action ('amal kathir). Scholars deduced five criteria, which are recorded in later Hanafi works. This lesson examines each opinion and highlights the authoritative view closest to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
Recognize that the primary texts lack a quantitative definition, so scholars derived criteria based on observation, custom, sequence, intention, and subjective assessment.
The absence of an explicit textual definition led to multiple valid opinions.
2
Enumerate the Five Opinions
List each opinion: (1) observer's doubt; (2) customary use of hands; (3) three consecutive movements; (4) intention of the doer; (5) the worshipper's own judgment.
These are distinct criteria, each with its own rationale.
3
Analyze Each Criterion
Understand the principle behind each opinion. For example, the second opinion uses 'urf (custom): actions normally done with both hands are excessive even if done with one, and vice versa.
Examples: putting on a turban (excessive) vs. removing a cap (little).
4
Identify the Preferred Opinion
According to al-Zayla'i (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq), the fifth opinion—deferring to the worshipper's assessment—is closest to Imam Abu Hanifa's view and is therefore mufta bihi.
This reflects the school's emphasis on the individual's state in worship.
5
Note the Flexibility
The Hanafi school does not impose a rigid numerical threshold but allows context and personal judgment, demonstrating a practical approach to fiqh.
This flexibility is consistent with the school's reliance on 'urf and the worshipper's own awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في الفاصل بينهما على خمسة أقوال: منها: أن لا يشك الناظر إليه أنه ليس في الصلاة، وإن اشتبه على الناظر فهو قليل على الأصح. والثاني: أن ما يقام باليدين عادة كثير وإن فعله بيد واحدة كالتعمم ولبس القميص ، وشد السراويل، وما يقام بيد واحدة قليل، وإن فعلــه باليدين، كنزع القميص وحل السراويل، ولبس القلنسوة، ونزعها ونزع اللجام. والثالث: الحركات الثلاث المتواليات كثير، وما دونه قليل، كحك موضع من جسده ثلاثاً ورمى ثلاثة أحجار، ونتف ثلاث شعرات، فإن كانت على الولاء تفسد وإلا فلا والرابع أن الكثير ما يكون مقصودا للفاعل والقليل خلافه. والخامس: أن يفوض إلى رأي المبتلى به وهو المصلي، فإن استكثره كان كثيراً وإن استقله كان قليلاً، قال الزيلعي وهذا أقرب الأقوال إلى رأي أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى.
And they differed about the distinction between them on five opinions: Among them: that the observer should have no doubt that he is not in prayer, and if it is ambiguous to the observer, it is little according to the most correct. The second: that what is normally done with both hands is much, even if he does it with one hand, like putting on a turban, wearing a shirt, tightening trousers; and what is done with one hand is little, even if he does it with both hands, like removing a shirt, loosening trousers, putting on a cap, taking it off, and removing a bridle. The third: three consecutive movements are much, and less than that is little, like scratching one spot on his body three times, throwing three stones, plucking three hairs; if they are consecutive, it invalidates, otherwise not. The fourth: that much is what is intended by the doer, and little is its opposite. The fifth: that it is referred to the opinion of the one afflicted, i.e., the praying person; if he considers it much, it is much, and if he considers it little, it is little. Al-Zayla'i said: This is the closest opinion to Abu Hanifa's view, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a definition-ruling that explains the five scholarly opinions on the criterion for excessive action ('amal kathir) in prayer. The issue is that the primary texts do not give a quantitative definition, so scholars deduced criteria. First opinion: The objective criterion—whatever makes an observer certain that the person is not praying is excessive; if uncertain, it is little. Second: Based on customary practice—actions normally done with both hands (like wearing a shirt) are excessive even if done with one hand, and vice versa. Third: Sequential movements—three consecutive motions are excessive; fewer than three are little. Fourth: Subjective intention—whatever the doer intends as a main activity (not part of prayer) is excessive. Fifth: The most authoritative view according to al-Zayla'i is to defer to the worshipper's own judgment, as they know their state best. This view is closest to Imam Abu Hanifa's position. These opinions show the Hanafi school's flexibility in applying the general rule.

Key Terms
العمل الكثير (al-'amal al-kathir): Excessive action in prayer; defined by the five opinions: observable doubt, customary use of hands, three consecutive movements, intention, or worshipper's judgment.
التعميم (al-ta'mim): Putting on a turban, cited as an example of action normally done with both hands, thus considered excessive even if done with one hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) By the worshipper's own assessment

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines excessive prayer action through multiple criteria, with the preferred view being the worshipper's own assessment, reflecting flexibility and individual focus.

Raising Hands (Raf' al-Yadayn) in Prayer: Disputed Ruling
Tanbīh (warning) on a debated practice with textual proof and critique of a weak narration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling on raising hands during prayer and explain why the weak narration attributing invalidation to Abu Hanifa is rejected.

Core Idea
Raising hands does not invalidate prayer; only excessive action that misleads an observer does.

Raising the hands at bowing and rising from bowing (raf' al-yadayn) is a debated practice across madhhabs. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi school's established position, drawing on textual evidence and the legal criterion for excessive action (al-ʿamal al-kathīr). A weak narration from Makḥul is examined and refuted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act in question
Confirm what rafʿ al-yadayn refers to: raising both hands to the ears during the bowing motion and when rising from it.
This is separate from the opening takbīr, where raising hands is agreed upon.
2
State the established Hanafi position
From al-Jāmiʿ and supported by al-Kāfī: it does not invalidate prayer; it is merely makrūh (disliked).
The passage explicitly says 'lā yufsid al-ṣalāh'.
3
Recognize the weak narration
A narration from Makḥul claims Abu Hanifa said it invalidates. The text labels it weak and cites the true criterion for invalidation.
This narration is rejected by later authorities.
4
Understand the criterion for excessive action
The defining test for invalidation is: 'if a far-off observer would think the person is not in prayer'. Raising hands fails this test.
This principle is drawn from al-Kāfī and others.

Warning: Raising the hands at bowing and raising from it does not invalidate prayer; it is explicitly stated in the chapter on Eid prayer in al-Jami'. And it is mentioned in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: the narration of Mak-hul from Abu Hanifa that it invalidates. Likewise in al-Fatawa al-Sughra. And al-Dayri said in his commentary: Mak-hul al-Nasafi related in a book called al-Shu'a' from Abu Hanifa that whoever raises his hands at bowing and raising from it, his prayer is invalidated, and he considered that excessive action because done with both hands. And it was criticized because the chosen view for excessive action is that if a person sees it from afar, he would think he is not in prayer. End. As we mentioned earlier. And he said in Sharh al-Munyah: The prayerful person should raise his hands at bowing and raising the head from it, because it is an extra action not from the perfections of prayer, and it does not invalidate prayer contrary to what Mak-hul narrated from Abu Hanifa that it invalidates, because the invalidator is only excessive action, which is what leads one to think the doer is not in prayer, and this raising is not like that. Mentioned in al-Kafi. End. Another warning: If a child suckles her breast himself and milk comes out, even with one suck, her prayer is invalidated; and it was corrected in al-Dirayah. And if milk does not come out, then three sucks are unanimously required. And in Majma' al-Riwayat, the correction of requiring three even when milk comes out is narrated: if she suckles once or twice, it does not invalidate; if more, it invalidates. And in al-Nawadir: milk came out for her, and that is the most correct. End. And by this an enigma is posed: a person drank, and the prayer of another was invalidated by his drinking, while he was not following him nor was he performing tayammum. A subtle branch: If one of the worshippers lifts himself from his place and then places it without turning from the qibla, his prayer is not invalidated; if he places it on an animal, it invalidates. Thus in al-Bahr. And it invalidates it: turning the chest from the qibla, because it involves abandoning the obligatory facing, unless he thinks
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage contains several supplementary rulings. First, a tanbih (warning) about raising the hands during bowing (raf' al-yadayn). The established Hanafi position is that it does not invalidate prayer, contrary to a weak narration from Mak-hul attributed to Abu Hanifa. The correct view is that raising hands is makruh (disliked) but not invalidating, since it does not make an observer think the person is not in prayer. Second, regarding a woman breastfeeding during prayer: if milk comes out, even with one suck, her prayer is invalidated. If no milk comes out, three consecutive sucks are needed to invalidate (by consensus). There is a dispute whether three are needed even when milk comes out; the preferred view is that one suck suffices if milk comes out. Third, a subtle branch (far' latif): if a worshipper lifts himself from his place and sets it down without turning from the qibla, prayer is not invalidated; but if he places himself on an animal (e.g., riding), it invalidates. Fourth, turning the chest away from the qibla invalidates prayer because it violates the obligatory direction. The passage ends mid-sentence about an exception if he thinks... but that is cut off.

Key Terms
رفع اليدين (raf' al-yadayn): Raising the hands to the ears during bowing and rising from bowing; a debated act in prayer.
العمل الكثير (al-ʿamal al-kathīr): Excessive action in prayer that would mislead an observer into thinking the person is not praying; such action invalidates prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is recommended (sunnah)
C) It is disliked but does not invalidate
D) It is obligatory

Key Takeaway
Raising hands in prayer is disliked but does not invalidate; the weak narration claiming otherwise is rejected based on the objective criterion for excessive action.

Breastfeeding, Moving Place, and Turning Chest: Actions Affecting Prayer Validity
Tanbīh (warning) with three rulings: breastfeeding, moving place, turning chest, each with its proof and indication of dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which actions related to breastfeeding, moving place, and turning the chest invalidate prayer according to the Hanafi school, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on breastfeeding.

Core Idea
Breastfeeding with milk invalidates prayer; moving without turning does not; turning the chest from qibla invalidates.

This lesson covers three distinct but often discussed scenarios that affect the validity of prayer: a woman breastfeeding during prayer, a worshipper shifting place without turning from the qibla, and turning the chest away from the qibla. The passage provides the Hanafi rulings and clarifies disputes, especially regarding the number of sucks needed for invalidation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three actions
The passage discusses: (1) breastfeeding (ridaʿ) during prayer, (2) moving one's place (tahwīl al-makān) without turning from the qibla, (3) turning the chest (tahwīl al-ṣadr) from the qibla.
Each has its own ruling based on whether the action is considered invalidating or not.
2
Analyze the breastfeeding ruling
If milk comes out, even with one suck, prayer invalidates (mufta bihi). If no milk comes out, three sucks are needed by consensus. There is a dispute whether three sucks are required even when milk comes out.
The preferred view is one suck with milk invalidates; the dispute is resolved by al-Dirayah.
3
Understand moving place without turning
If a person lifts himself from his spot and sits down again without deviating from the qibla, prayer is not invalidated. But if he sits on an animal (e.g., mounts a riding beast), it invalidates.
The key is preservation of direction; changing elevation (like mounting) is considered excessive.
4
Recognize turning the chest
Turning the chest away from the qibla invalidates prayer because it abandons the obligatory facing (tawajjuh).
This is agreed upon unless there is a genuine necessity (e.g., fear).

Source Text

Raising Hands at Rukoo' – Does It Invalidate Salah?
Refutation of a rare riwaya on hand-raising

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why raising hands at rukoo' does not invalidate salah according to the Hanafi school and evaluate the rare contrary riwaya.

Core Idea
Raising hands at rukoo' is makruh but does not break salah; only actions that make a distant observer think you are not praying are invalidating.

In this passage, the author clarifies a common point of confusion: does raising the hands during rukoo' invalidate the prayer? While a rare transmission attributes the opposite view to Abu Hanifa, the established Hanafi position relies on a precise definition of 'amal kathir. Let's trace the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on hand-raising at rukoo'
Identify the ruling: the prayer remains valid, but the action itself is disliked (makruh) because it is an extra action not part of the prayer's completion.
The passage explicitly states 'la yufsid al-salah' (does not invalidate the prayer).
2
Identify the rare opposing riwaya
A narration from Makhul al-Nasafi reports that Abu Hanifa considered it invalidating because it is done with both hands, thus constituting 'amal kathir (great action).
This riwaya is mentioned in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Fatawa al-Sughra.
3
Follow the Hanafi response: definition of 'amal kathir
The Hanafi school defines 'amal kathir as an action that, if seen by a distant observer, would cause them to think the person is not in prayer. Raising hands momentarily does not meet that criterion.
The observer test is the primary criterion, not merely the number of hands used.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: according to the majority Hanafi view, the prayer is valid; according to the rare riwaya, it would be invalid if done deliberately.
The majority view is relied upon for fatwa.
5
Note the tarjih (preponderance)
The non-invalidating position is the mufta bihi (operative ruling), as affirmed by al-Kafi, Sharh al-Munyah, and other authoritative sources.
The rare riwaya is considered weak and not acted upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه: رفع اليدين عند الركوع والرفع منه لا يفسد الصلاة، منصوص عليه في باب صلاة العيدين من «الجامع»، وذكر في «شرح الجامع الصغير» رواية مكحول عن أبي حنيفة أنه يفسد، كذا في «الفتاوى الصغرى». وقال «الديري» في شرحه: روى مكحول النسفي في كتاب سماه «الشعاع» عن أبي حنيفة: أن من رفع يديه عند الركوع والرفع منه تفسد صلاته، وجعل ذلك عملاً كثيراً حيث أقيم باليدين، ونظر فيه بأن المختار في العمل الكثير ما إن لو رآه شخص من بعيد ظنه ليس في الصلاة. انتهى، كما قدمناه. وقال في «شرح المنية»: المصلي ويكره أن يرفع يديه عند الركوع، وعند رفع الرأس منه، لأنه فعل زائد ليس من تتمات الصلاة، ولا يفسد الصلاة خلافاً لما روى مكحول عن أبي حنيفة أنه يفسدها؛ لأن المفسد إنما هو العمل الكثير، وهو ما يظن أن فاعله ليس في الصلاة، وهذا الرفع ليس كذلك، ذكره في «الكافي». انتهى.
A note: Raising the hands at the time of bowing and rising from it does not invalidate the prayer; it is textually established in the chapter on the two Eids in al-Jami'. And it is mentioned in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: a narration from Makhul from Abu Hanifa that it invalidates. Similarly in al-Fatawa al-Sughra. Al-Dayri said in his commentary: Makhul al-Nasafi narrated in a book he called al-Shu'a' from Abu Hanifa that whoever raises his hands at bowing and rising from it, his prayer is invalid, and he considered that a great action because it is done with both hands. But it was examined: the chosen view on great action is that if a person sees it from afar, he would think he is not in prayer. End quote, as we have mentioned. And it is said in Sharh al-Munyah: The worshiper should avoid raising his hands at bowing and at raising the head from it, because it is an extra action not part of the completion of prayer, but it does not invalidate the prayer, contrary to what Makhul narrated from Abu Hanifa that it invalidates; because that which invalidates is only the great action, which is that which makes the observer think its doer is not in prayer, and this raising is not like that. Mentioned in al-Kafi. End quote.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a tanbih (note) from the sharh addressing a contentious issue: raising the hands during rukoo' (including when going into rukoo' and rising from it). The author asserts the established Hanafi position that this action does NOT invalidate salah, even though it is disliked (makruh) because it is an unnecessary addition. The text cites multiple sources—al-Jami', Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Fatawa al-Sughra, and Sharh al-Munyah—to confirm that the majority Hanafi view is that it does not break the prayer. However, a rare narration from Makhul al-Nasafi reports that Abu Hanifa himself considered it invalidating, categorizing it as 'amal kathir because it is performed with both hands. The author refutes this by pointing out that the accepted definition of 'amal kathir is an action that would make a distant observer think the person is not in prayer; raising the hands momentarily does not meet that criterion. Thus, the correct position is that the prayer remains valid, though it is makruh to do so deliberately.

Key Terms
رفع اليدين (raf‘ al-yadayn): The act of raising both hands to the ears or shoulders, typically done at the beginning of salah (takbir) and sometimes at other postures.
تتمات الصلاة (tamāmāt al-ṣalāh): Actions that complete the prayer, i.e., its integral parts and recommended acts. Extra actions not from these are disliked.
الجامع (al-Jāmi‘): Refers to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's al-Jami' al-Saghir or al-Jami' al-Kabir, foundational Hanafi works.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon Hanafi ruling on raising hands at rukoo'?
A) It invalidates the prayer absolutely.
B) It is sunnah and recommended.
C) It does not invalidate but is disliked (makruh).
D) It is permissible and encouraged.

Key Takeaway
Raising hands at rukoo' does not invalidate salah; the criterion for 'amal kathir is the observer's perception, not merely the number of hands used.

Breastfeeding During Salah: When It Invalidates
Ruling on breastfeeding during salah with intra-madhhab dispute and riddle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breastfeeding during salah for two scenarios (with and without milk flow) and identify the conditions that invalidate the prayer, including the effect of moving from one's place.

Core Idea
One suck with milk flow invalidates; without flow, three sucks are needed—even a child's action can break your prayer.

This passage from Nūr al-Īḍāḥ explains a specific situation: a woman breastfeeding her child while performing salah. The ruling hinges on whether milk actually flows and the number of sucks. The text also presents a playful riddle (al-lughz) based on this ruling and gives a supplementary ruling about moving from one's place in salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between when milk flows from the breast and when it does not. The ruling changes accordingly: with flow, even one suck invalidates; without flow, three consecutive sucks are required.
2
Analyse the invalidating conditions
For milk flow: milk is najas and nullifies wudu, and the suckling is considered 'amal kathir (excessive movement). For no flow: three sucks constitute 'amal kathir and invalidate.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some transmissions (Majma' al-Riwayat) require three sucks even with milk flow, but the text declares the one-suck-with-flow view as the most correct (al-ashah), citing al-Nawadir.
4
Understand the riddle (al-lughz)
The riddle: a person drinks (the child), and the prayer of another (the woman) is invalidated, without her being an imam or in tayammum. The answer is the breastfeeding woman.
5
Apply the branch ruling on movement
Lifting oneself from the place without turning from the qibla does not invalidate, but placing oneself on a mount (significant movement) does.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبیه آخر إذا رضع صبي بنفسه ثديها فنزل اللبن ولو بمصة فسدت صلاتها، وصححه في «الدراية»: وإن لم ينزل اللبن فلابد من ثلاث مصات اتفاقاً، وفي «مجمع الروايات» نقل تصحيح اشتراط الثلاث مع نزول اللبن فقال: إذا ارتضع ثديها مرة أو مرتين لا تفسد، وإن زاد تفسد، وفي «النوادر»: ونزل لها لبن وهو الأصح انتهى. وبهذا يلغز فيقال: شخص شرب ففسدت صلاة غيره بشربه، ولم يكن مقتدياً به ولا متيماً. فرع لطيف: لو رفع أحد المصلين عن مكانه ثم وضعه من غير أن يحوله عن القبلة، لا تفسد صلاته، ولو وضعه على الدابة تفسد كذا في «البحر».
Another note: If a child suckles her breast by himself and milk flows, even with one suck, her prayer is invalidated; this is corrected in al-Dirayah. And if milk does not flow, then three sucks are necessary by agreement. In Majma' al-Riwayat, it is transmitted that the condition of three is required even with the flow of milk, and he said: If he sucks her breast once or twice, it does not invalidate; if he exceeds, it invalidates. And in al-Nawadir: milk flows for her, and that is the most correct view. End quote. And with this, a riddle is made: it is said: A person drank, and the prayer of another was invalidated by his drinking, while he was neither following him (in prayer) nor performing tayammum. A subtle branch: If one of the worshippers lifts from his place and then places it without turning from the qibla, his prayer is not invalidated; but if he places it on a mount, it invalidates. Thus in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific and somewhat unusual case: a woman breastfeeding a child while she is engaged in salah. The key ruling hinges on the amount of milk flow and the number of sucks. First, if the child sucks and milk actually comes out (even with one suck), the woman's salah becomes invalid because an action that is not part of the prayer has occurred, and the milk that exits is considered najas which invalidates wudu and thus the prayer. However, if no milk comes out, the action is considered additional movement; in that case, a single suck is not enough to invalidate—only three consecutive sucks constitute 'amal kathir and break the prayer. There is also a narration in al-Nawadir that even without milk flow, three sucks are required, and this is declared the most correct. The text then presents a playful riddle (لغز) based on this ruling: a person (the child) drinks, yet the prayer of another (the woman) is invalidated because of his drinking, without the woman being an imam or in a state of tayammum (i.e., she is praying normally). The answer is the breastfeeding woman. Finally, a related branch ruling: if a worshipper lifts himself from his place (e.g., moves a short distance) and then resumes without turning away from the qibla, his prayer is valid; but if he places himself on an animal (i.e., mounts a riding beast), that is a significant movement that invalidates the prayer.

Key Terms
مصة (maṣṣah): A single suck of the breast by an infant.
اللغز (al-lughz): A riddle or puzzle based on fiqh rulings, used to test knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling if a child suckles once but no milk comes out during the woman's salah?
A) Her salah is invalidated.
B) It is invalidated only if she intended to feed the child.
C) It is not invalidated unless three consecutive sucks occur.
D) It is invalidated only if the child is not her own.

Key Takeaway
A woman's salah becomes invalid if milk flows from even one suck, or if three consecutive sucks occur without milk flow; a single suck without flow is permissible.

Invalidators of Salah: Eating, Drinking, and Vocal Expressions
Detailed enumeration of invalidators with conditions and intra-madhhab differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the actions that invalidate salah according to the Hanafi madhhab and identify the conditions under which they do or do not invalidate.

Core Idea
Deliberate eating, drinking, and audible speech-like sounds invalidate salah unless unavoidable.

This lesson covers the key invalidators (mufsidat) of salah as detailed in the Hanafi commentary of Nur al-Idah. Understanding these rulings is essential for preserving the validity of one's prayer. The passage provides a comprehensive list with nuanced conditions and exceptions, particularly regarding unintentional actions and unavoidable circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalidating actions
From the passage, list the specific actions that invalidate salah: turning the chest from qibla, eating, drinking, clearing throat, saying 'Af/Tuf', groaning 'Ah', and saying 'Awah'.
Each action has its own conditions and exceptions.
2
Distinguish between deliberate and involuntary
For each action, note whether the ruling depends on intent or whether it invalidates even forgetfully. For example, eating and drinking invalidate even if forgotten, while clearing throat only invalidates if deliberate and produces letters.
The passage clarifies that intentionality is a key factor.
3
Analyze exceptions for excuses
Identify circumstances where otherwise invalidating actions are excused: turning for hadath (then clarified), clearing throat when compelled, or actions necessary in prayer of fear.
Exceptions are based on inability to avoid.
4
Understand the principle of audibility and letters
For throat clearing and expressions of pain, the passage emphasizes that if the sound produces clear letters (huruf muhajja), it invalidates; if not, it is disliked but valid.
This is a key Hanafi distinction.
5
Apply the rulings to new scenarios
Using the principles derived, evaluate hypothetical situations such as swallowing a small piece of food inadvertently or sighing loudly.
Application strengthens comprehension.

(And) invalidates it (turning the chest away from the qibla) because it involves abandoning the obligatory direction, except if he thought an event (hadath) occurred and turned away, then it becomes clear that it did not, before leaving the mosque, or passing the rows in the desert, and he went for purification to build upon his prayer due to prior hadath, or to align against the enemy in the prayer of fear. (And) invalidates it (eating something from outside his mouth, even if small) like a sesame seed, because it is possible to avoid it. (And) invalidates it (eating what is between his teeth) if it is abundant, and that abundant is the size of a chickpea, whether with little or much action; because it is possible to avoid it, contrary to eating a little with little action; because it is a follow-up to his saliva and cannot be avoided, and if it is with much action, it invalidates due to the presence of much action. (And) invalidates it (his drinking) because it contradicts the prayer, and there is no difference between deliberate and forgetful, as we have mentioned. If he raises his head to the sky and hail, snow, or rain falls into his throat and reaches his stomach, his fasting and his prayer are invalidated; because something external has reached his stomach. Thus in al-Bazzaziyyah, but in fasting it is conditioned that he be mindful of his fast. (And) invalidates it (clearing the throat without excuse, that he is not compelled to, and it produces letters; because speech is what is uttered. If it is with an excuse, like being compelled, it does not invalidate; because it is impossible to avoid, like a person with a cough or phlegm, and it is that he says: 'Ah' with fatha and damma, and the excuse comes from the one who has the right, so it is pardoned. And if no clear letters appear, it does not invalidate by agreement, but it is disliked without excuse, as in al-Bahr. And if he clears his throat to improve his voice and beautify it, his prayer is not invalidated according to the correct view, but it is invalidated according to al-Faqih Isma'il al-Zahid. And if the imam makes a mistake and the follower clears his throat to guide the imam, his prayer is not invalidated. And it is mentioned in al-Ghayah that clearing the throat to inform that he is in prayer does not invalidate, as in al-Tabyin, and he stated it unconditionally regarding the appearance of letters. And it is said in al-Tajnis: if he intends and his letters are heard, his prayer is invalidated. End quote. If the ruling in al-Ghayah is interpreted as referring to cases without letters, that would be a way of reconciliation, otherwise the contradiction is apparent. (And al-Ta'fif), whether he intends to blow dust and clean his prostration spot, or he intends annoyance, his prayer is invalidated if it is audible, otherwise not. And it is said in al-Dirayah from al-Mujtaba: He blew in the dust and said 'Af' or 'Tuf', it invalidates according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Yusuf, and the correct view is that the disagreement is regarding the light form, while the emphatic form invalidates by agreement. End quote. And some do not require the audible to have articulated letters; this is the view of Khwahirzadeh. (And al-Anin) which is that he says: 'Ah' – with sukun on the ha, shortened on the weight of da' – an expression of pain of the non-Arabs. (And al-Ta'awwuh) which is that he says: 'Awah', it is said: 'awaha' man ta'wihan and ta'awwaha ta'awwuhan if he said 'Awah', and it is a word of pain, and a man 'awwah' is one who often says 'Awah'. And it has many dialects: it may be with madd or without, with shaddah on the open waw, with sukun on the ha and kasr, and without ha, and with sukun on the waw and kasr on the ha, and other variations.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This lengthy passage from the sharh presents a comprehensive list of actions that invalidate salah, with detailed conditions and exceptions. It begins with turning the chest away from the qibla: if done deliberately, it invalidates because it abandons the obligatory direction. However, there are exceptions: if one thought he had a hadath (ritual impurity) and turned to check, then realized he did not, as long as he hasn't left the mosque, the prayer is not invalidated. Also, in the prayer of fear, moving to align against the enemy is excused. Next, eating or drinking anything from outside the mouth invalidates even if a tiny amount like a sesame seed, because it is avoidable. Eating food stuck between teeth invalidates only if the amount is substantial (size of a chickpea), regardless of the movement involved; a small amount that is swallowed with saliva is excused. Drinking invalidates absolutely, whether intentional or forgetful, because it negates the essence of prayer. If rain, snow, or hail falls into the throat, it invalidates both prayer and fast (with the condition of being mindful of the fast). Clearing the throat (tahanhuh) without excuse that produces audible letters invalidates because it constitutes speech; but if compelled (e.g., coughing or phlegm), it does not invalidate. Clearing to improve voice or guide the imam is generally non-invalidating on the correct view. Sighs and groans like 'Ah', 'Af', 'Tuf', and 'Awah' are also discussed: if audible and contain letters, they invalidate; if not, the prayer is valid but disliked. There is a disagreement among Hanafi scholars regarding audible expressions that do not form clear letters; some say they invalidate only if they produce letters, others consider any audible sound invalidating. The passage concludes with linguistic notes on the various forms of these exclamations.

Key Terms
التنحنح (al-tanaḥnuḥ): Clearing the throat deliberately, which if done without excuse and produces audible letters, invalidates salah.
التأفيف (al-ta’fīf): Saying 'Af' or 'Tuf' as an expression of annoyance or to blow dust; if audible, invalidates salah.
الأنين (al-anīn): Groaning or sighing in pain, saying 'Ah'; if audible and articulated, invalidates salah.
تحويل الصدر (taḥwīl al-ṣadr): Turning the chest away from the qibla, which invalidates salah unless for a valid excuse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It does not invalidate because clearing throat is not considered speech.

Key Takeaway
Knowing the precise invalidators of salah with their conditions ensures the worshiper can preserve the validity of prayer by avoiding deliberate actions that nullify it and understanding when exceptions apply.

Nullification of Prayer by Eating and Drinking
Commentary expanding matn rulings on eating/drinking nullifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on when eating or drinking nullifies prayer, distinguish between food from outside and between teeth, and apply the principle of imkan al-ihriaz (possibility of avoidance) to determine nullification.

Core Idea
Any avoidable intake during prayer nullifies it; food between teeth nullifies if the size of a chickpea or removed with much action.

Prayer requires complete focus and avoidance of worldly acts. Among the most common nullifiers is consuming food or drink. The Hanafi school provides detailed rulings based on the source of consumption and the amount, anchored in the principle of 'imkan al-ihriaz' (possibility of avoidance). Understanding these nuances helps you maintain the validity of your salah and avoid common pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of intake
List the three main categories mentioned: (1) food from outside the mouth, (2) food stuck between teeth, (3) drinking. Note also the special case of external elements like rain or snow.
2
Apply the principle of imkan al-ihriaz
For each category, ask: Could this intake have been avoided before starting the prayer? If yes, it nullifies. This is the core legal reasoning.
For food between teeth, avoidability depends on quantity and action.
3
Analyze the threshold for food between teeth
Distinguish between 'much' (size of chickpea) and 'little'. If much, it is avoidable and nullifies regardless of action. If little, it is inseparable from saliva and does not nullify (unless much action is used to swallow it).
The action needed to swallow also counts—if much action, it nullifies even with small food.
4
Consider the role of action
The text mentions 'whether with little or much action' for the chickpea-sized food. For small food, if you use much action (e.g., deliberate chewing or tongue movement), the excessive action itself nullifies the prayer.
The ruling is cumulative: either the food quantity or the action can be the effective cause.
5
Examine external elements and the ruling on forgetfulness
Rain, snow, hail falling into the throat also nullify prayer because they are external and avoidable. For prayer, there is no distinction between intentional and forgetful—any such intake nullifies. However, for fasting, forgetfulness is excused.
This difference between prayer and fasting is important for cross-topic understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأكلُ شيءٍ من خارج فَمِهِ، ولَوْ قَلَّ وأَكلُ ما بين أسنانِهِ، وهو قَدْرُ الحِمْصَةِ، وشُربُهُ (وَ) يفسدها (أَكْلُ شيءٍ من خارج فَمِهِ، ولَوْ قَلَّ) كسمسمة لإمكان الاحتراز عنه، (وَ) يفسدها (أكلُ ما بين أسنانه) إن كان كثيراً (وَهُوَ ) : أي: الكثير (قَدْرُ الحِمِّصَةِ) ، سواء كان بعمل قليل أو كثير؛ لإمكان الاحتراز عنه بخلاف أكــل القليل بعمل قليل؛ لأنه تبع لريقه ولا يمكن الاحتراز عنه، وإذا كان بعمل كثير يفسد بوجود العمل الكثير. (و) يفسدها (شُربُهُ) لأنه ينافي الصلاة، ولا فرق بين العمد والنسيان لما قدمناه، ولـو رفــع رأسه إلى السماء فوقع في حلقه برد، أو ثلج، أو مطر، ووصل إلى جوفه فسد صومه وصلاته؛ لوصول شيء من الخارج إلى جوفه، كذا في ( البزازية ، لكن في الصوم يشترط أن يكون ذاكراً لصومه.
And eating anything from outside his mouth, even if little, and eating what is between his teeth, and it is the size of a chickpea, and his drinking. (And) it nullifies (eating anything from outside his mouth, even if little) like a sesame seed, because it is possible to avoid it. (And) it nullifies (eating what is between his teeth) if it is much, and it (the much) is the size of a chickpea, whether with little or much action; because it is possible to avoid it, contrary to eating a little with little action; because it is incidental to his saliva and cannot be avoided. And if with much action, it nullifies due to the existence of much action. (And) it nullifies (his drinking) because it contradicts prayer, and there is no difference between intentional and forgetful, as we previously stated. If he raises his head to the sky and hail, snow, or rain falls into his throat and reaches his interior, both his fast and prayer are invalidated; due to the arrival of something from outside into his interior, as in al-Bazzaziyya, but regarding fasting it is conditioned that he be mindful of his fast.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage lists three related nullifiers of prayer under the general category of consuming external substances. The first is eating anything from outside the mouth, even a minute amount like a sesame seed, because such intake is entirely avoidable. The second is eating food stuck between one's teeth, but only if the amount is considerable—defined as the size of a chickpea (hummusa). This distinction is based on feasibility: a small amount mixed with saliva is inseparable and thus excused, whereas a large amount can be removed before prayer. If the act of removing it involves excessive movement (many actions), it nullifies regardless of quantity due to the excessive action itself. The third is drinking any liquid, which inherently contradicts the prayer's spirit of devotion. The ruling applies equally whether done intentionally or forgetfully, as established earlier. A special case is when external elements like rain or snow fall into the mouth unintentionally; this also nullifies prayer and fast because it constitutes external substance entering the body through the mouth. However, for fasting, the additional condition of 'mindfulness' (dhikr) is required—if one forgets and such intake occurs, the fast is not broken, unlike prayer where it is nullified regardless.

Key Terms
إمكان الاحتراز (imkan al-ihriaz): Possibility of avoidance; the legal principle that if one can avoid something, it invalidates the prayer.
الحمصة (al-himmisa): The size of a chickpea; the threshold for considering food between teeth as much (kathir).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It never nullifies as it is part of the mouth.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses 'imkan al-ihriaz' to rule that any avoidable intake during prayer nullifies, with a specific threshold for food between teeth and a special case for external elements.

Clearing the Throat During Prayer: Ruling on Nullification
Explaining the nullification of prayer by clearing the throat without excuse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which clearing the throat nullifies prayer and apply the Hanafi distinction between excuse and production of letters.

Core Idea
Clearing the throat nullifies prayer only if done without excuse AND produces distinct letters.

This passage clarifies a common confusion: not every throat-clearing is equal. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the presence of an excuse and whether the sound forms distinct letters. We will examine the ruling, its basis in the definition of speech, and the exceptions for coughs, guiding the imam, and improving voice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is clearing the throat (tanahnuh) during prayer. The default ruling is that it nullifies prayer, but only under specific conditions.
2
Determine the two conditions for nullification
It nullifies only if (1) done without an excuse (i.e., not compelled) and (2) it produces distinct letters (huruf). If either condition is missing, it does not nullify.
3
Analyse the dalil: definition of speech
The proof is that speech (kalam) is defined as 'what is uttered with letters' (ma tafaffaha bih). Since clearing the throat with letters produces audible utterances, it constitutes speech, which nullifies prayer.
This is a definitional argument based on urf/lughah.
4
Note exceptions and intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Exceptions include being compelled (e.g., cough, phlegm), guiding the imam, and, according to the sound view, improving one's voice. The sound view (mufta bihi) is that clearing throat to improve voice does not nullify, contrary to al-Faqih Isma'il al-Zahid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ يفسدها التَّنَحْنُحُ بلا عذر بأن لم يكن مدفوعاً إليه وحصل به حروف؛ لأن الكلام ما تلفظ به، ولـو كان بعذر بأن كان مدفوعاً إليه لا تفسد؛ لعدم إمكان الاحتراز عنه، كصاحب السعال، والبلاغـم. وهـو أن يقول: «أح» بالفتح والضم. والعذر جاء من قبل صاحب الحق فجعل عفواً، وإن لم يظهر به حروف مهجاة لا تفسد اتفاقاً، ولكنه مكروه بغير عذر كما في ( البحر ». ولو تنحنح لإصلاح صوته وتحسنه لا تفسد صلاته على الصحيح، وتفسد عند الفقيه إسماعيل الزاهد. ولو أخطـأ الإمام فتنحنـح المقتدي ليهتدي الإمام لا تفسد صلاته. وذكر في ( الغاية » : أن التنحنح للإعلام بأنه في الصلاة لا تفسد كما في « التبيين» كذا أطلقه عن قيد ظهور حروف، وقال في ( التجنيس» : إن تعمد وسمعت حروفه فسدت صلاته انتهى. فإن حمل ما في الغاية على ما ليس فيه حروف كان وجهاً للتوفيق بينهما، وإلا فالمخالفة ظاهرة.
And it nullifies clearing the throat without excuse, i.e., when he is not compelled to do it, and it produces letters, because speech is what is uttered. If it is with an excuse, i.e., he is compelled to do it, it does not nullify due to the impossibility of avoiding it, like one who has a cough or phlegm. It is to say 'Ah' with fatha or damma. The excuse comes from the right of the person (the one with the need) so it is excused. If no distinct letters appear, it does not nullify by agreement, but it is makruh without an excuse, as in al-Bahr. If he clears his throat to improve his voice, it does not nullify his prayer according to the sound view, but it nullifies according to al-Faqih Isma'il al-Zahid. If the imam makes a mistake and a follower clears his throat to guide the imam, it does not nullify his prayer. It is mentioned in al-Ghaya that clearing the throat to inform that one is in prayer does not nullify, as in al-Tabyin, but this is stated without the condition of producing letters. And in al-Tajnis it is said: if intentional and his letters are heard, his prayer nullifies. If what is in al-Ghaya is interpreted as cases without letters, that is a way to reconcile, otherwise there is apparent contradiction.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage discusses the nullifier of clearing the throat during prayer. The default ruling is that it nullifies if done without an excuse and produces distinct letters, because it constitutes speech. However, if there is an excuse such as cough or phlegm that compels the action, it is excused and does not nullify. The sound produced, like 'Ah' (with fatha or damma), is considered a form of speech if letters are heard. If no letters are distinguishable, it does not nullify by agreement but is discouraged (makruh) without excuse. There is a difference of opinion on clearing the throat to improve one's voice: the sound view says it does not nullify, but Faqih Isma'il al-Zahid holds it nullifies. When a follower clears his throat to correct the imam's mistake, it is permitted and does not nullify. The texts from al-Ghaya and al-Tajnis appear to conflict; the author suggests reconciliation by restricting al-Ghaya's ruling to cases without audible letters.

Key Terms
التنحنح (al-tanahnuh): Clearing the throat, producing a sound like 'ah'.
مدفوع إليه (madfu'un ilayh): Compelled to do it; an excuse that makes the action unavoidable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whenever it produces a sound.
B) Only if it produces distinct letters and is without excuse.
C) Only if it is done intentionally.
D) It never nullifies regardless of letters.

Key Takeaway
Clearing the throat nullifies prayer only when done without an excuse and producing distinct letters; exceptions exist for compulsion, guiding the imam, and (sound view) improving voice.

Sounds of Distress in Prayer: Ta'fif, Anin, and Ta'awwuh
Explains nullification of salah by distress sounds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on ta'fif, anin, and ta'awwuh during salah, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the light and heavy forms.

Core Idea
Audible sounds of distress — even single-letter groans — nullify salah if they constitute speech-like letters.

In Salah, any deliberate speech nullifies the prayer. But what about involuntary sounds of pain like 'uff', 'ah', or 'awh'? This passage from Imdad al-Fattah clarifies that even these sounds, if audible and composed of letters, break the prayer. Importantly, there is a nuanced disagreement within the Hanafi school regarding the light versus heavy pronunciation of such sounds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sounds
Note the three categories: ta'fif (uff/taf), anin (ah), and ta'awwuh (awh). Each is a sound expressing annoyance or pain.
2
Understand the condition of audibility
The key condition is that the sound must be audible (masmu'). If it is inaudible, the prayer remains valid.
Some scholars (Khawahirzada) do not require distinct letters, only audibility.
3
Distinguish light vs heavy forms
The text distinguishes between the light form (mukhaffaf, e.g., 'af' without emphasis) and the heavy form (mushaddad, e.g., with shaddah).
All agree the heavy form nullifies.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that even the light form nullifies. Abu Yusuf disagrees, saying it does not. The majority view is preferred.
The phrase 'al-sahih anna al-khilaf fi al-mukhaffaf' indicates the correct understanding.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view is that any audible sound with letters nullifies the prayer, regardless of lightness or heaviness.
This is the position of the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتَّأْفِيفُ) سواء أراد نفخ التراب وتنقية موضع سجوده، أو أراد التضجر فسدت صلاته إن كان مسموعاً، وإلا فلا. وقال في ( الدراية ) عن ( المجتبى» : نفخ في التراب، فقال: أف أو تف، فسدت عندهما، خلافاً لأبي يوسف، والصحيح أن الخلاف في المخفف، وفي المشدد تفسد بالاتفاق انتهى. ولبعضهم لا يشترط في المسموع أن يكون له حروف مهجاه، وإليه ذهب خواهـر زاده، (والأنين) وهو أن يقول: آه - بسكون الهاء مقصورة على وزن دع- توجع العجم، (والتأوه) وهـو أن يقول: أوه، يقال: أوه الرجل تأويهاً وتأوه تأوهاً إذا قال: أوه، وهي كلمة توجع، ورجل أواه كثير التأوه، وفيها لغات كثيرة، تمدُّ ولا تمدُّ مع تشديد الواو المفتوحة، وسكون الهاء وكسرها، وبـلا هـاء، وسكون الواو وكسر الهاء، وغير ذلك.
And al-ta'fif: whether he intends to blow dust and clean his prostration place or intends annoyance, his prayer nullifies if it is audible, otherwise not. It is said in al-Diraya from al-Mujtaba: If he blows in the dust and says 'af' or 'taf', it nullifies according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf. The correct view is that the disagreement is over the light form, but in the heavy form (shaddah) it nullifies by agreement. Some do not require the audible sound to have distinct letters, and this is the view of Khawahirzada. And al-anin: it is to say 'ah' with sukun on the ha, shortened like the word da', expressing pain like the non-Arabs. And al-ta'awwuh: it is to say 'awh'. It is said: awh al-rajul ta'weeh and ta'awwuh ta'awwuhan if he says 'awh', and it is a word of pain. A man is 'awwah' meaning one who sighs much. There are many dialects: with madd or without, with tashdid on the waw (fatha), with sukun on the ha or kasra, without ha, with sukun on the waw and kasra on the ha, and others.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage deals with three related sounds of distress during prayer: ta'fif (saying 'uff' or 'taf'), anin (groaning 'ah'), and ta'awwuh (sighing 'awh'). The ruling depends on audibility: if the sound is heard and constitutes distinct letters, the prayer nullifies. There is an intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Yusuf holds that the light form does not nullify, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say it does. All agree that the heavy form (with shaddah) nullifies. Some scholars, like Khawahirzada, do not require the sound to have distinct letters; any audible expression of pain suffices to nullify. The author provides linguistic notes on the various pronunciations of these words, emphasizing that they are considered speech if expressed audibly during prayer.

Key Terms
التأفيف (al-ta'fif): Saying 'uff' or 'taf' expressing annoyance or to blow dust.
الأنين (al-anin): Groaning, saying 'ah' in pain.
التأوه (al-ta'awwuh): Sighing, saying 'awh' in distress.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It nullifies according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf disagrees on the light form.

Key Takeaway
Audible sounds of distress with letters (ta'fif, anin, ta'awwuh) nullify salah according to the majority Hanafi view, with the only dispute being over the light form.

Crying During Prayer: Pain vs Piety
Distinguishing between crying from pain/calamity (nullifies) and from fear of Paradise/Fire (does not nullify)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing crying from worldly pain/calamity (nullifies) from devotional crying (does not nullify) and explain the underlying principle of dalala.

Core Idea
Crying from pain/calamity nullifies prayer; crying from piety enhances it.

The Hanafi school makes a critical distinction regarding crying in prayer: the cause matters. If one cries from worldly pain or calamity and produces letters, it invalidates the prayer because it conveys a message like explicit speech. However, crying from fear of the Hereafter or love of Allah increases khushu' and does not nullify prayer. This lesson examines the evidence and reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Legal Effect
Determine what action is being ruled upon (crying during prayer) and its potential effect on the prayer's validity.
The ruling depends on the cause and whether the crying produces letters.
2
Distinguish the Two Causes
Separate crying due to worldly pain/calamity from crying due to remembrance of Paradise/Fire. The former nullifies; the latter does not.
Worldly crying implies a request for help (like saying 'I am sick'), which is akin to speech.
3
Apply the Principle of Dalala
Understand the Hanafi maxim that an indication (dalala) which clearly conveys a message is treated like explicit speech (sarih) when no explicit speech contradicts it.
This principle equates crying from pain with explicit words of complaint.
4
Recognize the Exception for Khushu'
Crying from devotion enhances khushu' and is therefore excepted from nullification. This is agreed upon (ittifaqan) in the Hanafi school.
The hadith encouraging weeping in obedience supports this exception.
5
Evaluate the Evidence
Examine the hadith: 'Whoever obeys Allah while weeping will enter Paradise laughing...' as a proof that devotional crying is meritorious and not a nullifier.
The dalal is zanni but sufficient for establishing the ruling in the absence of a contrary text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و يفسدها ارتفاع بكائه وهو أن يحصل به حروف، وقوله: (من وجــع) بجسده (أو مصيبة) بفقد حبيب، أو مال متعلق بالأنين، وما بعده فهو قيد راجع للمسائل الأربع في الفساد؛ لأنه إذا حصل شيء منها لأحدهما صار كأنه يقول أنه مريض فاعذروه أو مصاب فعزوه، والدلالـة تعمل عمل الصريح إن لم يكن صريح يخالفها، ولو أفصح به تفسد ، فكذا هذا ، و ( لا ) تفسد بحصول هذه الأشياء الأنين وما بعده ( من ذكر جنة أو نار ) اتفاقاً لدلالته على زيادة الخشوع، والخاشع سمي بـه الخائف من الواحد القهار ، ولا لحم على بدنه، فكأنـه يـابس كـأرض خاشعة، وفي الحديث: « مَـنْ أطاع الله باكياً دخل الجنَّة ضاحكاً، ومن أذنب ذنباً ضَاحِكاً دَخَلَ النَّارِ بَاكِياً )
And it nullifies his crying, which is if it produces letters. And his saying: (from pain) in his body (or calamity) such as loss of a beloved or wealth, related to groaning. What follows is a condition referring to the four matters regarding nullification; because if any of these occurs to someone, it becomes as if he is saying 'I am sick, so excuse me' or 'I am struck, so console me'. Indication (dalala) operates like explicit speech if there is no explicit speech contradicting it. If he were explicit, it would nullify, so likewise this. And it does not nullify by the occurrence of these things (groaning and after) from remembrance of Paradise or Hell, by agreement, because it indicates increased khushu' (humility). The khashi' (humble one) is named from fear of the One, the Subduer; there is no flesh on his body, as if he is dry like a humble earth. And in the hadith: 'Whoever obeys Allah while weeping will enter Paradise laughing, and whoever commits a sin while laughing will enter Hell weeping.'
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage addresses crying during prayer. Crying due to worldly pain or calamity nullifies the prayer if it produces distinct letters, because it implies a request for empathy or help, which is akin to speech. The legal principle is that an indication (dalala) that conveys a message is treated like explicit speech (sarih) when no explicit speech contradicts it. Therefore, since explicitly stating 'I am sick' would nullify prayer, crying that clearly indicates the same also nullifies. However, crying from fear of Paradise or Hell is an exception; it does not nullify by agreement among Hanafi scholars, because it enhances khushu' (devotional humility). The hadith quoted encourages weeping in obedience to Allah, indicating that such crying is meritorious and not detrimental to prayer.

Key Terms
الخشوع (al-khushu'): Humility and devotion in prayer, increased by crying from fear of the Hereafter.
الدلالة تعمل عمل الصريح (al-dalala ta'malu 'amal al-sarih): The principle that an indication (like crying implying a message) is treated as explicit speech in legal rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The crying must produce distinct letters (huruf).
C) The crying must be accompanied by words.
D) Any audible crying nullifies.

Key Takeaway
The validity of prayer during crying hinges on the cause: worldly pain/calamity nullifies (if letters produced), while devotional crying from fear of the Hereafter does not nullify and even enhances prayer.

When Speaking During Prayer Nullifies: Intention Matters
Ruling on nullification by replies during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between permissible dhikr and nullifying speech during prayer based on intention, and apply the Hanafi position to cases where a worshiper responds to external speech.

Core Idea
Intention turns even Quranic phrases into nullifying speech if used as a reply during prayer.

Ever wondered if you can answer a question during prayer with a Quranic phrase? The Hanafi school teaches that intention is key. This lesson unpacks when such utterances nullify your prayer, clarifying an important intra-Hanafi difference on the role of wording vs. intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruled cases
From the passage, list the specific situations where a reply during prayer nullifies it: answering a question about Allah, responding to bad news, good news, and amazing news.
Each case involves a phrase that is otherwise permissible dhikr.
2
Understand the decisive principle
The passage states: 'speech is based on the intention of the speaker.' Learn that the same words can be worship or worldly speech depending on the speaker's goal.
This principle overrides the literal wording of the phrase.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the reply nullifies; Abu Yusuf disagrees for praise phrases. Compare their proofs: intent vs. fixed linguistic nature.
The example of calling a man named Yahya with a Quranic verse illustrates the majority's reasoning.
4
Apply the general rule
Any words, even resembling Quran, if intended as a reply to someone, nullify prayer. Practice recognizing scenarios where the intent is reply versus spontaneous dhikr.
The decisive factor is the speaker's intention at the moment of utterance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ يفسدها جواب مستفهم عن ند الله سبحانه: أي: قال قائل: هـل مـع الله إله آخر ؟ فأجابه المصلي بـ: ( لا إله إلا الله)، فسدت صلاته عندهما، خلافاً لأبـي يـوسـف هـو يقول: أنه ثناء بصيغته فلا يتغير بعزيمته، ولهما: أنه أخرجه مخرج الجواب، وهـو صـالـح لـه؛ لأنـه يستعمل في موضعه عرفاً فيجعل جواباً؛ لأن الكلام يبنى على قصد المتكلم، فإنَّ من رأى رجلاً اسمه يحيى وبين يديه کتاب فقال يا بـ خذ الكتاب بقوة، وأراد خطابه، لم يشكل على أحـد أنـه متكلم لا قارئ. ويفسدها جواب خبر سوء بالاسترجاع، وهو قوله: إنا لله وإنا إليـه راجعون. ويفسدها جواب مخبر بخبر سار بالحمد لله ) ، (وَ) يفسدها جواب خبر عجب بلا إله إلا الله أو جوابه بسبحان الله، و يفسدها كل شيء مما يشبه ألفاظ القرآن إذا قصـد بـه الجـواب
And it nullifies answering a questioner about a partner of Allah: i.e., someone said 'Is there another god with Allah?' and the praying person answered 'La ilaha illallah', his prayer nullifies according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf. He (Abu Yusuf) says: it is praise (thana') in its wording, so it does not change by his intention. But they (the two) say: he produced it as a reply, and it is suitable for that because it is used in that context customarily, so it is considered a reply; because speech is based on the intention of the speaker. For example, if one sees a man named Yahya and in front of him is a book and he says 'O Yahya, take the book with strength' intending to address him, it is not unclear to anyone that he is speaking, not reciting. And it nullifies responding to bad news with al-istirja', which is saying 'Inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un'. And it nullifies responding to glad tidings with 'Alhamdulillah'. And it nullifies responding to amazing news with 'La ilaha illallah' or with 'Subhanallah'. And it nullifies everything resembling Qur'anic words if intended as a reply.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage covers various instances of responding to external speech during prayer that nullify it. First, answering a question about Allah's oneness with 'La ilaha illallah' nullifies according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, even though it is a phrase of praise, because it is uttered as a reply. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the phrase remains praise regardless of intention. The author supports the majority view with an example: if someone sees a person named Yahya and says a Quranic verse addressing 'Yahya', it is clearly speech, not recitation, due to intention. Similarly, responding to bad news with 'Inna lillah', to good news with 'Alhamdulillah', or to amazing news with 'La ilaha illallah' or 'Subhanallah' all nullify if intended as replies. The general principle is that any words, even if resembling Quranic text, nullify prayer when used as a response to someone. The decisive factor is the intention: if the words are meant to communicate with another person, they constitute worldly speech and invalidate the prayer.

Key Terms
الاسترجاع (al-istirja'): Saying 'Inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un' upon hearing bad news.
ثناء (thana'): Praise; a form of dhikr that glorifies Allah.
جواب (jawab): Reply; speech directed at another person in response to their utterance.

Key Terms from Source Passage
