Ruling on Sujud al-Tilawa for the Masbuq
Ruling on masbuq following imam in sujud al-tilawa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two cases for the masbuq regarding sujud al-tilawa and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether returning invalidates the prayer.

Core Idea
A masbuq must follow the imam in sujud al-tilawa unless he has already prostrated; then returning is invalid according to the dominant view.

When the imam remembers a prostration of recitation during prayer, the masbuq faces a delicate situation. This ruling determines whether his prayer remains valid or is invalidated based on his choice to follow. Understanding this requires distinguishing between two scenarios and appreciating the internal Hanafi dispute that arises when the masbuq has already prostrated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct cases based on whether the masbuq has 'tied his rak'ah with a prostration' (i.e., performed sujud al-tilawa already) or not. Recognize the condition that triggers each ruling.
A masbuq is one who joins the prayer late and stands to complete his missed rak'ahs after the imam's taslim.
2
Apply the rule for scenario 1 (not yet prostrated)
If the masbuq has not yet prostrated in his rak'ah, he must return and follow the imam in sujud al-tilawa, then prostrate for forgetfulness (sajdah al-sahw). Failure to do so invalidates his prayer. Understand the reasoning: the imam's return nullifies the sitting for both, so the masbuq cannot separate.
3
Apply the rule for scenario 2 (already prostrated)
If the masbuq has already performed sujud al-tilawa in his rak'ah, there are multiple narrations. According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), if he returns to follow the imam, his prayer is invalid. If he does not return, there are two narrations: al-Asl says invalid, Nawadir says valid.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is that his prayer is invalid in either case, based on al-Asl.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Examine the reasoning behind the two positions: The narration of al-Asl argues that the imam's return nullifies the sitting, so the masbuq's independence occurred in a place of following, invalidating his prayer. Nawadir argues that the nullification does not extend to the masbuq because his independence was complete before the imam's return.
Tarjih: The later scholars (al-Bahr, al-Zahira) prefer al-Asl, which is from zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حكم سجود التلاوة فقال في « التجنيس» : لو تذكـــر الإمام سجدة تلاوة فسجدها، فإن كان المسبوق لم يقيد الركعة التي قام إليها بسجدة فعليه أن يعود إلى متابعة الإمام، فيسجد معه للتلاوة، ويسجد للسهو، ثم يسلم الإمام ويقوم المسبوق إلى قضاء ما عليه، ولا يعتد بما أتى به من قبل لما مرّ، ولو لم يعد فسدت صلاته؛ لأن عود الإمام إلى سجدة التلاوة يرفض القعدة في حق الإمام، وهو بَعدُ لم يصر منفرداً؛ لأن ما أتـي بـه دون فعل صلاة [ فتر تفض ] القعدة في حقه أيضاً [ فإذا ارتفضت في حقه ] فلا يجوز لـه الانفراد لوجوب المتابعة في أوانها، فيكون الانفراد مفسداً في هذه الحالة، وإن كان قد قيد ركعته بسجدة، فإن عــاد إلى متابعة الإمام فسدت صلاته رواية واحدة، وإن لم يعد ومضى عليها ففيها روايتان، ذكر في الأصل» أن صلاته فاسدة، قال في «البحر » : وهو «ظاهر الرواية كما في « المحيط ) وفي الظهيرية » : وهو أصح الروايتين انتهى. وذكر في نوادر أبي سليمان أنها لا تفسد صلاته. وجـه رواية الأصل: أن العود إلى سجدة التلاوة يرفض القعدة، فتبين أن المسبوق انفرد في موضع الاقتداء، فتفسد صلاته، ووجه « نوادر أبي سليمان»: أن ارتفاض القعدة في حق الإمام لا يظهر في حق المسبوق؛ لأن ذلك بالعود إلى سجود التلاوة، والعود حصل بعد ما تم انفراده عن الإمام وخرج عن متابعته، فلا يتعدى حكمه إليه كذا في ( البدائع »
As for the ruling of the prostration of recitation, it is said in al-Tajnisi: If the imam remembers a prostration of recitation and performs it, if the masbuq has not tied the rak'ah he stood for with a prostration, then he must return to following the imam, prostrate with him for recitation, and prostrate for forgetfulness, then the imam says salam, and the masbuq stands to make up what is due on him, and what he previously did is not counted. If he does not return, his prayer is invalid. Because the imam's return to the prostration of recitation nullifies the sitting in the imam's regard, and he has not yet become independent, because what he performed was without a prayer action, so the sitting is nullified in his regard as well, so when it is nullified in his regard, he is not permitted to be independent due to the obligation of following at that time, so independence becomes invalidating in this case. If he had already tied his rak'ah with a prostration, then if he returns to following the imam, his prayer is invalidated in one narration. If he does not return and continues, there are two narrations: it is mentioned in al-Asl that his prayer is invalid; al-Bahr says it is the apparent narration; and in al-Zahira: it is the more correct of the two narrations. And it is mentioned in Nawadir Abi Sulayman that his prayer is not invalidated. The reasoning for the narration of al-Asl: that returning to the prostration of recitation nullifies the sitting, so it becomes clear that the masbuq became independent in a place of following, so his prayer is invalid. The reasoning of Nawadir Abi Sulayman: that nullification of the sitting in the imam's regard does not appear in the masbuq's regard, because that is by returning to prostration of recitation, and the return occurred after his independence from the imam was complete and he left his following, so its ruling does not extend to him. Thus in al-Bada'i.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage discusses the masbuq's obligation to follow the imam in sujud al-tilawa (prostration of recitation). Two cases: (1) If the masbuq has not yet prostrated in the rak'ah he is making up, he must return and follow the imam; otherwise his prayer is invalid. (2) If he has already prostrated, there is a difference: the dominant view (zahir al-riwayah) says returning invalidates, while Nawadir Abi Sulayman says it does not. The dispute centers on whether the imam's return to sujud al-tilawa nullifies the sitting (and hence the masbuq's independence) in a way that affects the masbuq. The passage illustrates how Hanafi scholars weigh different narrations.

Key Terms
سجدة التلاوة (sajdat al-tilawa): Prostration performed upon reciting or hearing a verse of prostration in the Quran.
قيد ركعته بسجدة (qayyada rak'atahu bi-sajdah): To perform a prostration in that rak'ah, thereby completing it as a valid rak'ah.
مسبوق (masbuq): A latecomer who joins the congregation after the imam has begun, and must complete missed rak'ahs after the imam's salam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the masbuq has already prostrated in his rak'ah and the imam performs sujud al-tilawa, what is the ruling according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah)?
A) The masbuq must return and follow, and his prayer is valid.
B) If he returns, his prayer is invalid.
C) He may choose to return or not, both are valid.
D) He must not return under any circumstance.

Key Takeaway
The masbuq's obligation to follow the imam in sujud al-tilawa depends on whether he has already prostrated; if he has, the dominant view holds that any choice leads to invalidity.

Salah — Prayer
beginner
6 min
Invalidators: Not Repeating Final Sitting or Pillar Done Asleep
Two additional invalidators: not repeating final sitting after a missed sajdah and not repeating a rukn done while sleeping

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two invalidators of salah: failure to repeat the last sitting after a forgotten structural sajdah, and failure to repeat a rukn performed while asleep, and apply the ruling of invalidation to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
The last sitting is only valid after all pillars are complete; omitting its repetition after a structural sajdah invalidates the prayer.

This passage details two specific invalidators of salah: (1) forgetting to repeat the final sitting after a missed structural sajdah, and (2) not repeating a rukn performed while asleep. Both rulings underscore the principle that the prayer's sequence must be correct and performed with full consciousness. We will unpack the reasoning and identify the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts that invalidate the prayer
List the specific omissions: (1) Not repeating the last sitting after performing a structural sajdah that was remembered after the sitting. (2) Not repeating a pillar performed while asleep.
2
Understand the rationale for the first invalidator
The sitting is not considered valid until all pillars of that rak'ah are completed. A structural sajdah is a pillar; if it was missed and then performed after the sitting, the sitting is nullified.
3
Apply the ruling to sujud al-tilawa after the last sitting
Similarly, if one performs tilawa prostration after the last sitting, he must repeat the sitting because the tilawa prostration disrupts the sitting.
4
Understand the second invalidator: condition of being awake for pillars
A rukn performed while asleep is invalid because consciousness is a condition. Therefore, it must be repeated; otherwise the prayer is invalid.
5
Note the practical nuance regarding congregational prayer
If the imam forgets and the followers follow him, their prayers are also invalid if he abandons the sitting, but not those who did not follow him, as mentioned in al-Bazzaziyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ) يفسدها (عدم إعادة الجُلوس الأخير بعد أداءِ سجدةٍ صُلْبِيَّةٍ، تَذَكَّرَها بعدَ الجُلوس) لأنه لا يعتد بالجلوس، إلا بعد تمام الأركان لأنه يختمها، وكذا إذا سجد للتلاوة بعد القعود الأخير يلزمه إعادته؛ لأنه يرتفض بسجود التلاوة على المختار، كما ذكرناه، حتى لو سلم وعليه سجود تلاوة وتفرق القوم فتذكره في مقامه عاد إليـه وقعد، فإن ترك القعدة فسدت صلاته وصلاة من تابعه لا من لم يتابعه كذا في « البزازية». (And) it (the prayer) is invalidated by (not repeating the last sitting after performing a structural prostration that one remembered after the sitting) because the sitting is not counted except after completion of the pillars, as it concludes them. Similarly, if one prostrates for recitation after the last sitting, he must repeat the sitting, because it is nullified by the prostration of recitation according to the chosen view, as we mentioned. Even if he said salam and there was a prostration of recitation due on him and the people dispersed, then he remembered it in his place, he returns to it and sits. If he abandons the sitting, his prayer and the prayer of those who followed him are invalidated, but not those who did not follow him. Thus in al-Bazzaziyyah. (And) it (the prayer) is invalidated by (not repeating a pillar that one performed while sleeping, because a condition for its validity is being awake, as previously mentioned.

Commentary
This passage lists two more invalidators of prayer: (1) If a structural sajdah (sajdah sulbiyyah) is missed and remembered after the last sitting, the entire sitting must be repeated because the sitting is not valid until all pillars are complete. Similarly for sujud al-tilawa after the last sitting. (2) If a rukn (pillar) is performed while asleep, it must be repeated because the condition of being awake is essential. The text cites al-Bazzaziyyah for the first ruling.

Key Terms
سجدة صلبية (sajdah sulbiyyah): A structural prostration that is part of the regular sequence of a rak'ah, as opposed to sujud al-tilawa or sujud al-sahw.
ركن (rukn): An integral part of the prayer, the omission of which invalidates it unless compensated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must the last sitting be repeated after performing a structural sajdah that was remembered after the sitting?
A) The sitting becomes invalid because it was performed before the sajdah.
B) The sitting is not considered valid until all pillars are complete.
C) The sajdah itself invalidates the sitting.
D) The imam must repeat the sitting for the congregation.

Key Takeaway
Failure to repeat the last sitting after a missed structural sajdah, or failure to repeat a rukn performed while asleep, invalidates the prayer.

Latecomer and Imam's Sujda Tilawa After Final Sitting: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on latecomer with sujda tilawa after final sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the latecomer when the imam performs sujda tilawa after the final sitting, distinguish the two scenarios, and explain the preferred narration and its basis.

Core Idea
The latecomer's prayer is invalid (fasid) in the relied-upon Hanafi position when he fails to follow the imam's sujda tilawa after the final sitting.

This passage presents a nuanced scenario: the imam, after sitting for the final tashahhud, remembers a prostration of recitation (sujda tilawa) and performs it. The latecomer (masbuq), who is standing to complete his missed rak'ah, must decide whether to return to follow. The Hanafi school has two narrations, and the relied-upon position is that his prayer becomes invalid if he does not return, even if he has already prostrated. Understanding this ruling clarifies the obligation of following the imam and the principle that the imam's actions within the prayer still bind the latecomer until the imam's salām.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage describes two cases: (a) the latecomer has not yet performed the prostration (sajda) in his own rak'ah; (b) he has already performed the sajda. Distinguish their rulings.
2
Analyze the obligation in scenario (a)
In the first scenario, the latecomer must return to follow the imam, perform sujda tilawa and sajda sahw, then complete his prayer. Failure to return invalidates his prayer.
3
Examine the dispute in scenario (b)
For the latecomer who already prostrated, there are two narrations: from al-Aṣl (invalid) and Nawādir Abī Sulaymān (valid). Understand the reasoning of each.
4
Evaluate the reasoning behind each narration
Al-Aṣl argues that the imam's return nullifies the sitting (qa'dah) for both imam and latecomer, so the latecomer's separation is invalid. Nawādir argues that the nullification occurs after the latecomer's separation, so it does not affect him.
Focus on the timing of nullification and the obligation to follow.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text clearly states that the narration of al-Aṣl is the apparent (ẓāhir al-riwāyah) and the sounder (aṣaḥḥ). Thus, the relied-upon position is that the prayer is invalid.
Al-Baḥr, al-Muḥīṭ, and al-Ẓahīriyya all affirm this preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حكم سجود التلاوة فقال في « التجنيس» : لو تذكـــر الإمام سجدة تلاوة فسجدها، فإن كان المسبوق لم يقيد الركعة التي قام إليها بسجدة فعليه أن يعود إلى متابعة الإمام، فيسجد معه للتلاوة، ويسجد للسهو، ثم يسلم الإمام ويقوم المسبوق إلى قضاء ما عليه، ولا يعتد بما أتى به من قبل لما مرّ، ولو لم يعد فسدت صلاته؛ لأن عود الإمام إلى سجدة التلاوة يرفض القعدة في حق الإمام، وهو بَعدُ لم يصر منفرداً؛ لأن ما أتـي بـه دون فعل صلاة [ فتر تفض ] القعدة في حقه أيضاً [ فإذا ارتفضت في حقه ] فلا يجوز لـه الانفراد لوجوب المتابعة في أوانها، فيكون الانفراد مفسداً في هذه الحالة، وإن كان قد قيد ركعته بسجدة، فإن عــاد إلى متابعة الإمام فسدت صلاته رواية واحدة، وإن لم يعد ومضى عليها ففيها روايتان، ذكر في الأصل» أن صلاته فاسدة، قال في «البحر » : وهو «ظاهر الرواية كما في « المحيط ) وفي الظهيرية » : وهو أصح الروايتين انتهى. وذكر في نوادر أبي سليمان أنها لا تفسد صلاته. وجـه رواية الأصل: أن العود إلى سجدة التلاوة يرفض القعدة، فتبين أن المسبوق انفرد في موضع الاقتداء، فتفسد صلاته، ووجه « نوادر أبي سليمان»: أن ارتفاض القعدة في حق الإمام لا يظهر في حق المسبوق؛ لأن ذلك بالعود إلى سجود التلاوة، والعود حصل بعد ما تم انفراده عن الإمام وخرج عن متابعته، فلا يتعدى حكمه إليه كذا في ( البدائع»
As for the ruling of the prostration of recitation, he said in al-Tajnīs: If the imam remembers a prostration of recitation and performs it, then if the latecomer had not tied the rak'ah he stood for with a prostration, he must return to following the imam, prostrate with him for the recitation, and prostrate for forgetfulness, then the imam gives salām and the latecomer rises to complete what he owes, and he does not count what he previously did due to what has passed. If he does not return, his prayer is invalidated because the imam's return to the prostration of recitation nullifies the sitting in the imam's right, and he has not yet become separate, because what he did does not constitute an act of prayer — the sitting is nullified in his right as well, so separation is not permissible due to the obligation of following at that moment, so separation invalidates in this case. If he had tied his rak'ah with a prostration, then if he returns to following the imam, his prayer is invalidated in one narration. If he does not return and proceeds, there are two narrations: in al-Aṣl it is mentioned that his prayer is invalid; al-Baḥr said it is the apparent narration, as in al-Muḥīṭ and al-Ẓahīriyya: it is the sounder of the two narrations. It is mentioned in Nawādir Abī Sulaymān that it does not invalidate his prayer. The basis of the narration of al-Aṣl is that returning to the prostration of recitation nullifies the sitting, so it becomes clear that the latecomer separated in a place of following, so his prayer is invalid. The basis of Nawādir Abī Sulaymān is that the nullification of the sitting in the imam's right is not apparent in the latecomer's right, because that nullification occurs by returning to the prostration of recitation, and the return happened after his separation from the imam and exit from following, so its ruling does not extend to him, as in al-Badā’i‘.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the latecomer (masbuq) when the imam remembers a prostration of recitation (sujda tilawa) after the final sitting (qa'dah). The author cites al-Tajnīs which presents two scenarios. First scenario: the latecomer has not yet performed the prostration (sajda) in the rak'ah he stood up for. In this case, he must return to following the imam by prostrating with him for the recitation and then for forgetfulness (sahw). The imam then gives salām, and the latecomer rises to complete his missed rak'ahs. He does not count what he did previously because the imam's return to sujda tilawa nullifies the sitting (qa'dah) in both the imam's and the latecomer's right. If the latecomer does not return, his prayer is invalid. Second scenario: the latecomer had already performed the prostration in his own rak'ah. If he returns to follow the imam, his prayer is invalid in one narration. If he does not return and continues, there are two narrations. The narration of al-Aṣl (Zahir al-Riwayah) says his prayer is invalid, and this is the sounder (aṣaḥḥ) narration according to al-Baḥr, al-Muḥīṭ, and al-Ẓahīriyya. The narration in Nawādir Abī Sulaymān says it does not invalidate his prayer. The reasoning for al-Aṣl is that the imam's return nullifies the sitting, so the latecomer is effectively separating from the imam when he should follow, thus invalidating his prayer. The reasoning for Nawādir is that the nullification occurs after the latecomer has already separated from the imam (by standing for his own rak'ah), so it does not affect him. Thus, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that his prayer is invalid.

Key Terms
المسبوق (al-masbūq): The person who joins the congregational prayer late, missing one or more rak'ahs with the imam.
سجود التلاوة (sujūd al-tilāwah): A prostration performed upon reciting or hearing a verse of sajdah in the Quran during prayer.
القعود الأخير (al-qu‘ūd al-ākhir): The final sitting in prayer, after the last prostration, which includes the recitation of al-Tashahhud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) ruling in the Hanafi school regarding the latecomer (masbūq) who already performed the prostration in his own rak'ah when the imam returns to perform sujda tilawa after the final sitting, and the latecomer does not return to follow the imam?
A) His prayer is valid because he had already separated from the imam.
B) His prayer is invalid according to Zahir al-Riwayah.
C) His prayer is valid only if he was unaware of the imam's forgetfulness.
D) His prayer is invalid only if he intentionally did not return.

Key Takeaway
In the relied-upon Hanafi view, the latecomer who already prostrated in his own rak'ah must not return to follow the imam's sujda tilawa after the final sitting; if he does not return, his prayer is invalid.

Salah — Prayer

Effect of Imam's Post-Sitting Invalidity on the Latecomer
Intra-Hanafi dispute on masbūq's prayer validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of the imam's qahqaha or intentional impurity after the final sitting on the masbūq's prayer, and explain why Abu Hanifa's view differs from that of the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The latecomer's prayer is built on the imam's; an invalid basis makes the building invalid.

This lesson explores a nuanced ruling within the Hanafi school: what happens to the prayer of a latecomer (masbūq) when his imam laughs loudly or intentionally breaks wudu after the final sitting but before saying salām? There is a disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding their reasoning reveals how the concept of 'building' (binā') works and why the latecomer's prayer is uniquely vulnerable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The question is whether the masbūq's prayer becomes invalid when the imam commits an act that would normally invalidate prayer (qahqaha or intentional impurity) after the final sitting.
The imam's own prayer is not invalidated by these acts after the final sitting; the ruling applies only to the follower.
2
State Abu Hanifa's view
Abu Hanifa holds that the masbūq's prayer is invalid. His reasoning: qahqaha and intentional impurity invalidate the part of the imam's prayer in which they occur. Since the masbūq's remaining prayer is built on that part, the building becomes invalid.
The key is binā’: the latecomer builds his remaining prayer on the portion he prayed with the imam.
3
State the Sahibayn's opposition
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that the masbūq's prayer remains valid. They draw analogy with salām, speech, and leaving the mosque, which do not invalidate the follower's prayer after the final sitting. Since the imam's prayer is not invalid, the follower's should not be either.
The Sahibayn emphasize the general rule that the follower's prayer follows the imam's in validity and invalidity.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa distinguishes the cases: salām ends the prayer and is commanded; speech is similar to salām; leaving the mosque is commanded by the Quran. But qahqaha and intentional impurity are inherently invalidating acts. Moreover, the imam and the mudrik (one who caught the whole prayer) do not need to build, but the masbūq does. Building on an invalid part is invalid.
The distinction hinges on whether the act is a commanded part of prayer or an external invalidator.
5
Determine the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf: according to Abu Hanifa, the latecomer must repeat his prayer; according to the Sahibayn, he does not. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa.
In practice, a latecomer whose imam laughs after the final sitting would need to repeat his prayer according to the Hanafi fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبها قهقهة إمام المسبوق) وإن لم يتعمدها، وحَدَثُهُ العَمْدُ) الحاصل بغير القهقهة أيضـاً إذا قهقه أو أحدث عمداً (بعد الجُلوس الأخير قدر التشهد عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما؛ لأن صلاة المقتدي مبنية على صلاة الإمام صحة وفساداً، ولم تفسد صلاة الإمام فكذا صلاته، كالسلام والكـــلام والخروج من المسجد، وله أن القهقهة والحدث العمد مفسدان للجزء الذي يلاقيانه من صلاة الإمام، فيفسدان مثله . من صلاة المقتدي، غير أن الإمام والمدرك لا يحتاجان إلى البناء، والمسبوق ومن حاله مثل حاله يحتاج إليه، والبناء على الفاسد فاسد، بخلاف السلام لأن الصلاة تنتهي بـه، وهـو مـن واجباتها مأمور به، والكلام في معناه لأن السلام كلام لوجود كاف الخطابة فيه، والخروج من المسجد من موجبات التحريمة؛ لكونه مأموراً به لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِذَا قُضِيَتِ الصَّلَوةُ فَانتَشِرُوا فِي الأَرْضِ ) [ال : ١٠]،
And it invalidates (the prayer): loud laughter of the imam of the latecomer, even if unintentional, and his intentional impurity occurring by other than laughter as well, if he laughs or intentionally becomes impure after the final sitting to the extent of the tashahhud according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two companions. Because the follower's prayer is built on the imam's prayer in validity and invalidity, and the imam's prayer was not invalidated, so likewise his prayer, like salām, speech, and leaving the mosque. His (Abu Hanifa's) argument is that loud laughter and intentional impurity invalidate the part of the imam's prayer that they encounter, so they invalidate its equivalent from the follower's prayer, except that the imam and the one who catches up (mudrik) do not need to build (binā’), while the latecomer and one in his situation need to build, and building on something invalid is invalid, contrary to salām because the prayer ends with it, and it is among the obligations commanded, and speech is in its meaning because salām is speech due to the presence of the addressing kāf, and leaving the mosque is among the necessities of the taḥrīmah because it is commanded by His statement: 'And when the prayer is concluded, disperse within the land' (62:10).
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage addresses the invalidation of the latecomer's (masbūq) prayer when the imam laughs loudly (qahqaha) or intentionally commits an impurity (ḥadath al-amd) after the final sitting (qa'dah akhir) up to the extent of the tashahhud. There is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad). According to Abu Hanifa, these acts invalidate the latecomer's prayer, while according to the Sahibayn, they do not. The Sahibayn argue that the follower's prayer is built on the imam's prayer; since the imam's prayer is not invalidated by these acts (because the prayer is already complete after the final sitting), the follower's prayer should also not be invalidated. They draw analogy with salām, speech, and leaving the mosque, which do not invalidate the follower's prayer. Abu Hanifa responds by distinguishing: qahqaha and intentional impurity invalidate the part of the imam's prayer that they occur in, and by extension invalidate the equivalent part of the follower's prayer. The difference with salām is that salām ends the prayer and is commanded; speech (kalām) is similar because salām is also speech; leaving the mosque is commanded by the Quran. The imam and the mudrik (one who caught the whole prayer) do not need to build (binā’), but the latecomer needs to build his remaining prayer on what he already prayed. If the imam's prayer became invalid at any point, then the latecomer's prayer, which is built on it, becomes invalid. Hence, the latecomer's prayer is invalidated by the imam's qahqaha or intentional impurity after the final sitting. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
قهقهة (qahqaha): Loud, uncontrollable laughter that produces a sound like 'qah qah'.
المسبوق (al-masbūq): A person who joins the congregational prayer late, missing one or more rak'ahs.
البناء (al-binā’): Building one's prayer upon a previous part; the latecomer builds his remaining prayer on the part he prayed with the imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the reason for the invalidation of the latecomer's prayer when the imam laughs after the final sitting?
A) The laughter invalidates the imam's entire prayer.
B) The latecomer's prayer is built on the imam's prayer, and the laughter invalidates the portion it occurs in.
C) The latecomer must maintain his own wudu separately.
D) The laughter removes the obligation to complete the prayer.

Key Takeaway
The latecomer's prayer is invalidated by the imam's post-final-sitting qahqaha or intentional impurity because the building (binā’) on an invalid part is itself invalid according to Abu Hanifa, the relied-upon view.

Premature Salām After Two Rak'ahs: Ruling and Conditions
Ruling on premature salām after two rak'ahs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on premature salām after two rak'ahs in non-two-rak'ah prayers and identify the conditions that lead to invalidation.

Core Idea
Premature salām in non-thunā’iyya prayers invalidates even if due to mistaken assumptions or ignorance.

In this passage, the author explains that intentionally giving salām after two rak'ahs in prayers that are normally more than two rak'ahs — such as Maghrib, Ẓuhr, or ‘Ishā’ — invalidates the prayer. This ruling holds consistently even when the worshipper mistakenly thinks he is a traveler, or believes the prayer is Friday or Tarāwīḥ, or is ignorant of the ruling. Understanding this helps you avoid a common invalidator of prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is giving salām after completing two rak'ahs in a prayer that is not inherently two rak'ahs (thunā’iyya). The ruling: the prayer becomes invalid (fasid).
2
Recognize the essential condition for invalidation
The salām must be intentional (amd) and done with the conviction (qaṭ‘) that the prayer is complete. This distinguishes it from forgetfulness, which would require sajda sahw without invalidation.
If done forgetfully, the prayer is not invalid, but sajda sahw is needed.
3
Examine the mistaken assumptions that do not excuse
The text lists several scenarios: thinking you are a traveler (when you are not), thinking it is Jumu‘a, thinking it is Tarāwīḥ, or being a recent convert/ignorant and believing the fard prayer is two rak'ahs. In all these cases, the prayer is invalid.
The invalidation is not excused by ignorance of the ruling or the facts.
4
Apply the ruling to a concrete scenario
Take a resident who, after two rak'ahs of Ẓuhr, gives salām because he thinks he is a traveler. According to the text, his prayer is invalid and must be repeated in full.
This is because the act is intentional premature salām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبها السلام على رأس ركعتين في غير الثنائية) الفجر والمقصورة بالسفر وغيرها المغرب ورباعية المقيم (ظانّاً أنَّه مسافر) ولم يكن مسافراً (أو) ظاناً (أنَّها الجمعة أو) ظاناً ( أنَّها التراويح، وهي) أي: التي صلاها الظهر و العشاء، أو كان قريب عهد بالإسلام) أو جاهلاً نشأ مسلماً، فظنَّ الفرض ركعتين) في غير الثنائية؛ لأنه سلام عمـد عـلـى جهة القطـع قبل أوانه فيفسد الصلاة.
And it invalidates (the prayer): salām after two rak'ahs in other than two-rak'ah prayers — i.e., Fajr and the shortened prayer in travel, and others like Maghrib and the four-rak'ah of the resident — assuming that he is a traveler and he is not, or assuming that it is Friday, or assuming that it is Tarāwīḥ, and it (the prayer he performed) is Ẓuhr or ‘Ishā’, or he is a recent convert to Islam or an ignorant person raised as a Muslim, and thus he thought the obligatory prayer is two rak'ahs in other than a two-rak'ah prayer, because it is intentional salām on the basis of certainty before its time, so it invalidates the prayer.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage states that giving salām after two rak'ahs in a prayer that is not inherently two rak'ahs (ghayr al-thunā’iyya) invalidates the prayer. The prayer is invalidated when the worshipper mistakenly assumes a reason that would make the prayer two rak'ahs. The examples given: (1) thinking he is a traveler but he is not — a traveler shortens the four-rak'ah prayers to two, so if a resident thinks he is a traveler and gives salām after two, it invalidates. (2) Thinking the prayer is Friday (Jumu‘a) which is two rak'ahs, but it is actually Ẓuhr. (3) Thinking he is praying Tarāwīḥ (which is two rak'ahs each unit), but it is ‘Ishā’. (4) Being a recent convert or ignorant person who was raised Muslim but mistakenly believes the obligatory prayer is two rak'ahs. In all these cases, the salām is considered intentional (amd) and done with the conviction (qaṭ‘) that the prayer is complete, but it is premature. Since it is a voluntary act of cutting the prayer before its proper completion, it invalidates the prayer. This does not apply to an unintentional salām due to forgetfulness, which would require sajda sahw.

Key Terms
الثنائية (al-thunā’iyya): Prayers that are inherently two rak'ahs, such as Fajr and the shortened prayer of a traveler (qasr).
السلام على رأس ركعتين (al-salām ‘alā ra’s rak‘atayn): Giving salām after completing two rak'ahs, prematurely ending the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a resident gives salām after two rak'ahs of Ẓuhr thinking he is a traveler?
A) The prayer is valid because he had a valid intention to shorten.
B) The prayer is invalid because he is not a traveler and he intentionally ended the prayer early.
C) The prayer is valid but he must perform sajda sahw.
D) The prayer is valid if he was ignorant of the travel distance rules.

Key Takeaway
Intentional premature salām in non-thunā’iyya prayers invalidates the prayer, regardless of mistaken beliefs or ignorance.

Invalidators of the Masbuq's Prayer: Laughter, Hadath, and Premature Salam
Fiqh ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute and commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three specific invalidators of a masbuq's prayer and analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the imam's laughter and intentional hadath after the final sitting.

Core Idea
A masbuq's prayer is built on the imam's; corruption in the imam's prayer after its completion can invalidate the masbuq's prayer, but only for certain actions like laughter and hadath.

This lesson examines three actions that, when performed by the imam or the masbuq himself, specifically invalidate the prayer of a masbuq (latecomer). The passage from Nur al-Idah with its commentary clarifies the ruling and highlights a notable difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding whether the imam's laughter or intentional hadath after the final sitting passes its invalidity to the masbuq's prayer. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of building (bina') in congregational prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three invalidators
List the three actions that invalidate the masbuq's prayer according to the matn: (1) laughter of the masbuq's imam, even unintentional; (2) intentional hadath of the imam after the final sitting to the extent of tashahhud; (3) salam at the end of two rak'ahs in prayers that are normally more than two, under mistaken assumptions (e.g., thinking it's a two-rak'ah prayer when it is not).
The first two are debated between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn; the third is a separate ruling.
2
Understand the principle of building (bina')
The masbuq's prayer is built upon the imam's prayer in validity and invalidity. If the imam's prayer becomes corrupted at any point, the masbuq's prayer that depends on that part also becomes corrupt. However, the imam's prayer may not be invalid overall if the corruption occurs after its completion.
This principle is central to the dispute.
3
State Abu Hanifa's view and his dalil
Abu Hanifa holds that the masbuq's prayer is invalidated by the imam's laughter or intentional hadath after the final sitting. His reasoning: these actions corrupt the part of the imam's prayer that the masbuq is building upon, and building upon something corrupt is invalid. The imam's prayer is not invalidated because the corruption occurred after completion, but the masbuq still has prayer to complete.
4
State the Sahibayn's view and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the masbuq's prayer remains valid. They analogize to cases where the masbuq says salam, speaks, or leaves the mosque after the imam, which do not invalidate. Since the imam's prayer overall is valid, the masbuq's prayer built on it is also valid.
5
Analyze the third invalidator: premature salam
If the masbuq (or any worshiper) intentionally says salam after two rak'ahs in a prayer that is normally more than two (e.g., Dhuhr, 'Asr, Maghrib, 'Isha) due to a mistaken belief (travel, Jumu'ah, Tarawih, or recent conversion), his prayer is invalidated because he terminated the prayer prematurely with intent.
This does not involve a dispute; it is a clear ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وَ) يفسدها (قهقهة إمام المسبوق) وإن لم يتعمدها، وحَدَثُهُ العَمْدُ) الحاصل بغير القهقهة أيضـاً إذا قهقه أو أحدث عمداً (بعد الجُلوس الأخير قدر التشهد عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما؛ لأن صلاة المقتدي مبنية على صلاة الإمام صحة وفساداً، ولم تفسد صلاة الإمام فكذا صلاته، كالسلام والكـــلام والخروج من المسجد، وله أن القهقهة والحدث العمد مفسدان للجزء الذي يلاقيانه من صلاة الإمام، فيفسدان مثله . من صلاة المقتدي، غير أن الإمام والمدرك لا يحتاجان إلى البناء، والمسبوق ومن حاله مثل حاله يحتاج إليه، والبناء على الفاسد فاسد، بخلاف السلام لأن الصلاة تنتهي بـه، وهـو مـن واجباتها مأمور به، والكلام في معناه لأن السلام كلام لوجود كاف الخطابة فيه، والخروج من المسجد من موجبات التحريمة؛ لكونه مأموراً به لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِذَا قُضِيَتِ الصَّلَوةُ فَانتَشِرُوا فِي الأَرْضِ ) [ال : ١٠]، (و) يفسدها (السّلامُ على رأس ركعتين في غير الثنائية) الفجر والمقصورة بالسفر وغيرها المغرب ورباعية المقيم (ظانّاً أنَّه مسافر) ولم يكن مسافراً (أو) ظاناً (أنَّها الجمعة أو) ظاناً ( أنَّها التراويح، وهي) أي: التي صلاها الظهر و العشاء، أو كان قريب عهد بالإسلام) أو جاهلاً نشأ مسلماً، فظنَّ الفرض ركعتين) في غير الثنائية؛ لأنه سلام عمـد عـلـى جهة القطـع قبل أوانه فيفسد الصلاة.
And the laughter of the imam of the masbuq, and his intentional hadath after the final sitting, and the salam at the end of two rak'ahs in other than the two-rak'ah prayers, thinking he is a traveler, or that it is Jumu'ah, or that it is Tarawih and it is Isha, or he is a recent convert to Islam, so he thought the obligatory prayer is two rak'ahs. (And) it (the prayer) is invalidated by (the laughter of the imam of the masbuq) even if he did not intend it, and his intentional hadath (occurring other than laughter) also, if he laughs or intentionally commits hadath after the final sitting to the extent of tashahhud according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (companions); because the prayer of the follower is built upon the prayer of the imam in validity and invalidity, and the imam's prayer was not invalidated, so likewise his prayer, like salam, speech, and leaving the mosque. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that laughter and intentional hadath invalidate the part that they encounter from the imam's prayer, so they invalidate the like of it from the follower's prayer, except that the imam and the mudrik do not need to rebuild, while the masbuq and whoever is like him need to rebuild, and building upon something invalid is invalid, contrary to salam because prayer ends with it, and it is among its obligations and is commanded, and speech is analogous because salam is speech due to the presence of the direct address particle, and leaving the mosque is among the requirements of the tahrimah because it is commanded by His statement: 'And when the prayer is concluded, disperse in the land' (al-Jumu'ah:10). (And) it (the prayer) is invalidated by (salam at the end of two rak'ahs in other than the two-rak'ah prayers) - Fajr and the shortened prayer during travel and other than them, Maghrib and the four-rak'ah of the resident - (thinking he is a traveler) and he is not, (or thinking) it is Jumu'ah, (or thinking) it is Tarawih, and it (the prayer he performed) is Dhuhr and Isha, (or he is a recent convert to Islam) or an ignorant person raised as a Muslim, (so he thought the obligatory prayer is two rak'ahs) in other than the two-rak'ah prayer; because it is intentional salam with the intention of termination before its time, so it invalidates the prayer.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage expands on three specific invalidators of prayer that apply to a masbuq (latecomer). The first is the laughter of the imam of the masbuq, even unintentional. The second is intentional hadath (nullification of wudu) after the final sitting to the extent of tashahhud, according to Abu Hanifa. The third is salam at the end of two rak'ahs in prayers that are normally more than two rak'ahs, under mistaken assumptions about travel or the nature of the prayer. The commentary explains an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa holds that these invalidate the masbuq's prayer because his prayer is built on the imam's, and since the imam's prayer was partially corrupted, the masbuq's prayer is built on that corrupted part, making it invalid. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, arguing that since the imam's prayer itself was not invalidated (since the imam completed the prayer), the masbuq's prayer remains valid, similar to cases of salam, speech, and leaving the mosque. Abu Hanifa distinguishes those cases by noting that salam and speech are completions or commands, whereas laughter and hadath corrupt the very part of the prayer that the masbuq latches onto, and building on the corrupt is corrupt.

Key Terms
قهقهة (qahqaha): Laughter in prayer that invalidates it; specifically, audible laughter with sound.
حدث عمد (hadath 'amd): Intentional nullification of wudu, such as passing wind or urine, during prayer.
جلوس أخير (julus akhir): The final sitting in prayer, after the last sajdah, for tashahhud and salam.
مسبوق (masbuq): A latecomer who joins the prayer after the imam has started, missing one or more rak'ahs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following actions does NOT invalidate a masbuq's prayer according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), even though it might according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The imam laughs intentionally after the final sitting.
B) The masbuq says salam after two rak'ahs in Dhuhr prayer thinking it is Fajr.
C) The imam passes wind intentionally after the final sitting.
D) The masbuq stands up and leaves the mosque after the imam's salam.

Key Takeaway
The masbuq's prayer is uniquely vulnerable to invalidation by certain actions of the imam that do not affect the imam's own prayer, due to the principle of building, and this is subject to an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Actions That Do Not Invalidate Prayer – Hanafi Rulings
Lists actions that do not invalidate prayer despite being potentially inappropriate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state four actions that do not invalidate prayer, identify the conditions for each exemption, and explain the underlying Hanafi principle of takhfif (ease).

Core Idea
Prayer is not invalidated by looking at writing, eating a small residual, a passerby, or a glance at a revocably divorced woman's private part – the law embeds ease.

This passage from 'Nur al-Idah' presents four situations that might be thought to break prayer but do not. The Hanafi school applies a principle of communal ease (takhfif) to exempt certain actions that are difficult to avoid or are trivial. Understanding these rulings helps you distinguish between what truly invalidates prayer and what is merely disliked or sinful for other reasons.

Guided Path
1
Identify each scenario
Read the passage and list the four distinct actions: looking at a written text and understanding it, eating residual food less than a chickpea, a passerby crossing the prostration place, and a man looking at the inner private part of his revocably divorced wife with desire.
2
Determine the ruling for each
For each scenario, confirm that the prayer is not invalidated (la tafsud). Note that some actions are disliked (makruh) or sinful for the passerby, but the prayer remains valid.
E.g., looking at writing is makruh tanzih; eating residual is makruh; passing is sinful for the passer but not invalidating; looking at the private part does not invalidate according to the chosen view.
3
Analyze the reasons (dalil)
The passage cites a hadith: 'Nothing cuts off the prayer…' for the passerby. For the other cases, reasons include lack of speech, difficulty of avoidance, and the smallness of the action. Identify the underlying principle of takhfif (ease).
4
Note the conditions and limits
Each exemption has conditions: for eating, the food must be less than a chickpea and require little action; for the passerby, the prohibition zone is the place of prostration (with varying opinions on size); for the private part, only the inner part and with desire, but looking is permissible because the woman is still his wife (revocable divorce).
5
Apply the principle to new scenarios
Use the same reasoning of takhfif to evaluate similar borderline actions, e.g., swallowing a small piece of sugar that melts without chewing, or a child walking in front of the prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وإنْ أَثِـم الـمـار) المكلف لقوله : ( لَوْ يَعْلَمُ الْمَارُ بَيْنَ يَدَيْ الْمُصَلِّي مَاذَا عَلَيْهِ لَكَانَ أنْ يَقِفَ أرْبَعِينَ خَيْرًا لَهُ مِنْ أَنْ يَمُرَّ بَيْنَ يَدَيْهِ » ) رواه الشيخان، وفي رواية البزار : « أربعين خريفاً ) ) . وإنما يكره إذا مر في موضع سجوده في الأصح إذا كان المسجد كبيراً؛ لأن هذا القدر حقه، وفي تحريم ما وراءه تضييق عـلـى الـمـارة، وقيل: بقدر صفين، وقيل: بقدر ثلاثة أذرع، أو بخمسة، أو بأربعين، وأما في المسجد الصغير فيكــره مطلقاً). والصحراء كالكبير ولو كان المصلي على دكان) قدر قامة الرجل لا بأس به، وإن أقل كــره لمحاذاة بعض أعضائه، ولا تَفْسُدُ) صلاة الرجل (بنَظَرِهِ إِلى فَرْجِ المُطَلَّقَةِ) يعني: فـرجـهـا الـداخــل (بشهوة، في المُخْتَارِ) كذا في ( الخلاصة؛ لأنه عمل قليل، والنظر إليها حلال؛ لأن الطلاق الرجعي
Chapter on what does not invalidate prayer:
(If the praying person looks at a written text and understands it) whether it is Quran or other, intending to understand or not, he has violated etiquette but the prayer is not invalidated due to the absence of speech. Or (if he eats what is between his teeth, and it is less than a chickpea, without much action) it is disliked but does not invalidate due to the difficulty of avoiding it and it becoming like his saliva. And he restricted (by what is between his teeth) excluding if there was a sugar candy in his mouth and something melted from it and reached his throat, then it invalidates his prayer. And if he swallowed it before entering the prayer, but he finds its sweetness during the prayer, it does not invalidate. Or (if a passerby passes in front of his prostration place, it does not invalidate) whether the passerby is human, dog, woman, or donkey, due to his (peace be upon him) saying: 'Nothing cuts off the prayer, and ward off as much as you can, for it is only a devil.' And we will mention its completion later if Allah wills. (And the passerby is sinful) if morally responsible, due to his saying: 'If the one who passes in front of a praying person knew what is upon him, it would be better for him to stand for forty than to pass in front of him' narrated by the two (Bukhari and Muslim). And in the narration of al-Bazzar: 'forty autumns.' And it is only disliked if he passes in the place of prostration, according to the most correct view, if the mosque is large, because this amount is his right, and forbidding beyond that would constrain the passersby. And it is said: to the extent of two rows, and said: to the extent of three cubits, or five, or forty. As for a small mosque, it is disliked absolutely. And the open space is like a large mosque, even if the praying person is on a bench, the height of a man's stature is fine, and if less, it is disliked due to the alignment of some of his limbs. And (the prayer of a man is not invalidated by him looking at the private part of a revocably divorced woman) meaning her inner private part, with desire, according to the chosen view, as in al-Khulasa, because it is a small action, and looking at her is permissible because the revocable divorce...
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This section presents four situations that do not invalidate prayer but may be disliked or sinful. First, looking at a written text and understanding it, even if not reciting, does not invalidate because speech is absent, but it is against propriety. Second, eating residual food smaller than a chickpea with minimal action is excused due to common difficulty, but larger pieces or sugar that melts invalidate. Third, a passerby in front does not cut the prayer for the praying person, but the passerby commits a sin. The author discusses different opinions on the forbidden zone: the place of prostration is the limit, varying with mosque size. Fourth, looking at the inner private part of a revocably divorced woman with desire does not invalidate because it is a small action and looking is permissible since she is still his wife. This is the chosen view.

Key Terms
مكتوب (maktub): A written text that the praying person looks at and understands during prayer.
حمصة (himmisa): A chickpea, used as a measure for the size of food particles that are excused if less than that.
مرور (murur): Passing in front of a praying person, specifically in the place of prostration.
فرج المطلقة الرجعية (farj al-mutallaqah al-raj'iyyah): The private part (inner genitalia) of a woman who is in a revocable divorce period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) It is disliked but does not invalidate the prayer.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the default is that prayer is not easily invalidated; minor actions like looking at writing, eating a small residual, a passerby, or glancing at a revocably divorced wife's private part do not break the prayer, reflecting the principle of takhfif.

Actions That Do Not Invalidate the Prayer (Part 1)
Listing actions that do not break prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three actions that do not invalidate prayer according to the Hanafi school and explain the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Looking at writing, eating a chickpea-sized morsel, or a passerby crossing the prostration area do not break prayer — though they may be disliked or sinful.

This lesson examines specific actions that, while they may seem disruptive, do not nullify the prayer according to the Hanafi school. Understanding these rulings helps avoid unnecessary doubt and clarifies the precise boundaries of prayer invalidation. The passage from Imdad al-Fattah outlines three such actions and provides hadith evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify each action and its ruling
List the three actions: looking at writing, eating a small morsel, and a passerby crossing. Note the ruling for each: not invalidating (sahih) but may be makruh or sinful.
The prayer remains valid; the actions are not nullifiers.
2
Examine the exception for eating
Understand the condition: eating less than a chickpea from between the teeth, without much action, does not invalidate. But if a candy melts and reaches the throat, it invalidates.
The exception shows the ruling is tied to the source and manner of consumption.
3
Analyze the hadith proofs
The first hadith states 'Nothing cuts off the prayer' proving no external factor nullifies. The second hadith shows the passerby sins but does not affect validity.
Both hadith are ahad but the meaning is clear (qati al-dalala).
4
Consider the extent of the prostration area
The commentary explains that in a large mosque only the prostration spot is protected; in a small mosque it is always disliked to pass. The desert is like a large mosque.
The spatial ruling varies by context.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that a woman, dog, or donkey passing invalidates prayer. Hanafis reject this based on the explicit hadith.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; each school has its own evidence.

Source Text

فصل فيما لا يفسد الصلاة ( لو نظر المصلِّي إلى مكتوب وَفَهمَهُ) سواء كان قرآناً أو غيره، قصد الاستفهام أو لم يقصد، خالف الأدب ولا تفسد؛ لعدم النطق بالكلام، أو أكل ما بين أسنانه، وكان دون الحمصة، بلا عمل كثير) كره، ولا تفسد لعسر الاحتراز عنه وصيرورته كريقه، واحترز بما بين أسنانه عـمـا لـو كـان في فمه سكرة فذاب منها شيء ووصل إلى حلقه، فإنه يفسد صلاته، ولو ابتلعها قبل دخوله في الصلاة، ولكنه وجد حلاوتها في الصلاة لا تفسد ، أو مر مارٌ في موضع سجودِهِ، لا تفسد)، سواء كان المـــار آدمياً أو كلباً أو امرأة أو حماراً، لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: « لا يقطعُ الصَّلاةَ شيء وادرؤوا ما استطعتُم، فإنَّما هو شيطان» (١) وسنذكر تمامه(٣) إن شاء الله تعالى. (وإنْ أَثِـم الـمـار) المكلف لقوله : ( لَوْ يَعْلَمُ الْمَارُ بَيْنَ يَدَيْ الْمُصَلِّي مَاذَا عَلَيْهِ لَكَانَ أنْ يَقِفَ أرْبَعِينَ خَيْرًا لَهُ مِنْ أَنْ يَمُرَّ بَيْنَ يَدَيْهِ » ) رواه الشيخان، وفي رواية البزار : « أربعين خريفاً ) ) . وإنما يكره إذا مر في موضع سجوده في الأصح إذا كان المسجد كبيراً؛ لأن هذا القدر حقه، وفي تحريم ما وراءه تضييق عـلـى الـمـارة، وقيل: بقدر صفين، وقيل: بقدر ثلاثة أذرع، أو بخمسة، أو بأربعين، وأما في المسجد الصغير فيكــره مطلقاً). والصحراء كالكبير ولو كان المصلي على دكان) قدر قامة الرجل لا بأس به، وإن أقل كــره لمحاذاة بعض أعضائه،
Chapter on what does not invalidate the prayer. (If the praying person looks at a written text and understands it), whether it is Quran or other, intending to understand or not, he violates propriety but it does not invalidate; because there is no utterance of speech. Or he eats what is between his teeth, and it is less than a chickpea, without much action: it is disliked and does not invalidate due to difficulty of avoiding it and it becoming like his saliva. The exception to 'between his teeth' is if there is a candy in his mouth and something from it melts and reaches his throat, then his prayer is invalidated. If he swallowed it before entering prayer but tastes its sweetness during prayer, it does not invalidate. Or if a passerby passes in front of him, in his place of prostration, it does not invalidate, whether the passerby is a human, dog, woman, or donkey, due to his (peace be upon him) statement: 'Nothing cuts off the prayer, and ward off as much as you can, for it is a devil.' We will mention its completion later, if Allah wills. (And the passerby sins) if he is accountable, due to his statement: 'If the passerby in front of the prayerer knew what he incurs, standing for forty would be better for him than passing in front of him.' narrated by the two Shaykhs, and in the narration of al-Bazzar: 'forty autumns.' And it is disliked only if he passes in the place of prostration in the correct view if the mosque is large, because this amount is his right, and prohibiting beyond it would be a restriction on passersby. It is said: the amount of two rows, or three cubits, or five, or forty. As for a small mosque, it is disliked absolutely. And the desert is like a large mosque. If the prayerer is on a platform the height of a man's stature, there is no harm; if less than that, it is disliked due to alignment of some of his limbs.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on things that do not invalidate prayer enumerates three actions that, despite being disapproved or sinful, do not nullify the prayer. First: looking at a written text and understanding it. This is makruh (disapproved) because it involves disengagement from the prayer, but it does not invalidate since the person does not speak. Second: eating something from between the teeth that is less than a chickpea, without much movement. The Sharh explains the exception: if one has a candy in the mouth that melts and goes down the throat, the prayer is invalidated. But if the candy was swallowed before prayer and only the taste remains, it is not invalidated. Third: a passerby passing in front of the prayerer in the place of prostration does not invalidate the prayer. However, the passerby commits a sin if he is legally responsible. The author cites hadith to prove that nothing cuts off the prayer (meaning it remains valid) and that passing incurs sin. The commentary also discusses the extent of the area considered in front of the prayerer in different mosque sizes and open spaces, noting the preferred view that in a large mosque only the immediate sujud area is protected, while in a small mosque it is always disliked. The overall ruling is that the prayer remains valid (sahih), though the specific actions may be makruh or sinful.

Key Terms
الحمصة (al-ḥimṣa): A chickpea; used as a measure for the maximum amount of food that can be eaten without invalidating prayer.
المار (al-mārr): The passerby who walks in front of a praying person within the area of prostration.
موضع السجود (mawḍiʿ al-sujūd): The area where the praying person prostrates, which is the protected zone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person praying has a piece of candy in his mouth that melts and goes down his throat. What is the ruling on his prayer according to the Hanafi school?
A) The prayer remains valid.
B) The prayer is invalidated.
C) The prayer is valid but he must perform sajda sahw.
D) The prayer is valid only if the candy was less than a chickpea.

Key Takeaway
These actions do not nullify the prayer, but the praying person should avoid them to maintain proper etiquette.

Looking at a Divorced Wife's Private Parts During Prayer
Ruling on looking and physical contact with revocably divorced wife during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on looking at a revocably divorced wife during prayer and explain the qiyas that differentiates between looking and kissing/touching.

Core Idea
Looking does not invalidate prayer; kissing and touching do, because they resemble intercourse.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah discusses a nuanced ruling: a man's prayer remains valid even if he looks at his revocably divorced wife's private parts with desire. The reason is that looking is a small action and she is still his wife during the waiting period. However, if he kisses or touches her, the prayer is invalidated because these actions fall under the legal category of 'in the sense of intercourse' (fi ma'na al-jima'), which is a major nullifier. The text also addresses a gender asymmetry: a woman's prayer is invalidated only if she feels desire from the kiss or touch, while a man's prayer invalidates regardless of his own desire.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary act and its ruling
Find the main ruling: a man looking at his revocably divorced wife's private part with desire during prayer. The ruling is that the prayer is not invalidated (sahih).
This is the chosen view (al-mukhtar) from later Hanafi works.
2
Understand the basis for not invalidating
Two reasons are given: (1) looking is a small action (amal qalil), and (2) looking at her is lawful because revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse, so it is not a haram look.
The wife remains legally a wife during the iddah.
3
Identify what invalidates and why
Kissing and touching invalidate the prayer because they are 'in the sense of intercourse' (fi ma'na al-jima'), and intercourse is a major nullifier. This is based on qiyas (analogy).
The text cites al-Tajnis wa al-Mazid and al-Bazzaziyya.
4
Analyze the gender asymmetry
For a man, kissing his wife (even without desire) invalidates his prayer because his action is analogous to intercourse. For a woman, her prayer invalidates only if the kiss/touch causes her desire or if her husband desires.
The reasoning: desire is stronger in women, and intercourse is the husband's act.
5
Apply the qiyas structure
Map the analogy: asl (intercourse) → far' (kissing/touching) → illa (being in the sense of intercourse) → hukm (invalidates). See the qiyas panel for details.
The objection about gender is answered by noting that the woman's action is not analogous unless she desires.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تَفْسُدُ) صلاة الرجل (بنَظَرِهِ إِلى فَرْجِ المُطَلَّقَةِ) يعني: فـرجـهـا الـداخــل (بشهوة، في المُخْتَارِ) كذا في ( الخلاصة؛ لأنه عمل قليل، والنظر إليها حلال؛ لأن الطلاق الرجعي لا يحرم الوطء، (وإن ثبت به الرَّجعةُ)، ولو قبلها المصلي، أو لمسها فسدت صلاته؛ لأنه في معنى الجماع، والجماع عمل كثير، كذا في « التجنيس والمزيد » ، وفي ( البزازية »: جامعها زوجها بين الفخذين فيها فسدت صلاتها، وإن لم ينزل، وكذا إذا قبلها بشهوة، أو بغير شهوة، أو لمسها بشهوة؛ لأنه في معنى الجماع، بخلاف ما إذا قبلته ولم يشتهها ، ولو نظر إلى فرج مطلقته فـيـهـا صـار مراجعاً، أو نظر حتى ثبتت حرمة المصاهرة لا تفسد الصلاة في المختار، انتهى. (١) فإن قلت ما الفرق بين تقبيلها إياه أو لمسها وهو يصلي بغير شهوة منه وبين تقبيله إياها أو لمسه وهي تصلي بغير شهوة أيضاً حيث تفسد صلاتها لا صلاته قلت الفرق أن الشهوة فيهنّ أبلغ فتقبيله مستلزم لاشتهائها. وأيضاً تقبيله مطلقاً ومسه بشهوة في معنى الجماع يعني والجماع فعل الزوج ففعله الدواعـي كـفـعـلـه حقيقـة الجماع، ولو جامعها ولو بين الفخذ تفسد صلاتها فكذا هذا بخلاف المرأة فإن الجماع ليس فعلها فلا يكون إتيانها بالدواعي في معنى الجماع ما لم يشته الزوج. ط.
And the prayer of a man is not invalidated (by his looking at the private part of his divorced wife) meaning her interior private part (with desire, according to the chosen view) as in al-Khulasa; because it is a small action, and looking at her is lawful since revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse, (though it establishes the right to take back). If the praying person kissed her or touched her, his prayer is invalidated because it is in the sense of intercourse, and intercourse is a major action. Thus in al-Tajnis wa al-Mazid, and in al-Bazzaziyya: If her husband had intercourse with her between her thighs, her prayer is invalidated, even if he did not ejaculate. Similarly, if he kissed her with desire, or without desire, or touched her with desire, because it is in the sense of intercourse, unlike if she kissed him and he did not desire her. And if he looked at the private part of his divorced woman, he becomes a revoker, or if he looked until the prohibition of affinity is established, the prayer is not invalidated in the chosen view. End. (1) If you say: What is the difference between her kissing him or touching him while he prays without desire from him, and between him kissing her or touching her while she prays without desire also, where her prayer is invalidated but not his? I say: The difference is that desire in women is stronger, so his kissing necessarily entails her desire. Also, his kissing absolutely and his touching with desire is in the sense of intercourse, meaning intercourse is the husband's action, so his engaging from motives is like the reality of intercourse. If he had intercourse with her even if between the thighs, her prayer is invalidated. So this is similar, unlike the woman, because intercourse is not her action, so her engaging from motives is not in the sense of intercourse unless the husband desires. End.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific ruling: A man praying while looking at the private part of his revocably divorced wife with desire does not invalidate his prayer. The reason given is that it is a small action (qalil), and looking at her is lawful because revocable divorce does not prohibit sexual intercourse; he can take her back. However, if he kisses or touches her, the prayer is invalidated because that is in the sense of intercourse, which is a major nullifier (naqid). The text then discusses a significant intra-Hanafi issue: the asymmetry between a man and a woman regarding kissing and touching during prayer. While a man's kissing or touching his wife (with or without desire) invalidates his prayer because it resembles intercourse (which is his act), a woman's prayer is invalidated only if her husband kisses her and she desires it, or if he touches her with desire. The reasoning is that for a woman, the act of intercourse is not her action; only when the husband feels desire does her action become analogous to intercourse. This shows the Hanafi school's use of qiyas (analogy) based on the effective cause (illa) of being 'in the sense of intercourse'. The ruling is presented as the chosen view (al-mukhtar), indicating it is the relied-upon position in later Hanafi fatwa collections.

Key Terms
المطلقة (al-muṭallaqa): A woman who has been divorced, specifically with a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i).
الطلاق الرجعي (al-ṭalāq al-rajʿī): A revocable divorce that allows the husband to take his wife back without a new marriage contract.
في معنى الجماع (fī maʿnā al-jimāʿ): An action that is similar to intercourse in legal effect, though not actual copulation.
عمل قليل (ʿamal qalīl): A small action that does not break the prayer, as opposed to a major action (ʿamal kathīr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer is invalid because looking with desire is a major nullifier.
B) The prayer is valid because looking is a small action and the wife is still lawful to him.
C) The prayer is invalid only if he ejaculates.
D) The prayer is valid only if he looks without desire.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a man's prayer remains valid if he looks at his revocably divorced wife's private part with desire, but kissing or touching invalidates the prayer because these actions are analogous to intercourse.

Asymmetric Invalidation of Prayer by Spousal Touch
Distinguishes male and female in prayer invalidation by touch

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of a husband's touch on his wife's prayer, explain the asymmetric reasoning using the concept of 'fi ma'na al-jima'', and apply the rule to a new scenario.

Core Idea
A husband's touch invalidates his wife's prayer; her touch does not invalidate his — because intercourse is the husband's act and women are more prone to desire.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah addresses a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: the asymmetric effect of spousal touch during prayer. While you might assume that a kiss or touch affects both spouses equally, the Hanafis derive a differential ruling based on the nature of sexual desire and the attribution of intercourse. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning (qiyas) and reveals the underlying principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the asymmetry
Notice that the passage distinguishes between two scenarios: when the husband touches/kisses his wife while she prays, and when the wife touches/kisses her husband while he prays. The ruling differs.
Focus on the phrase 'تفسد صلاتها لا صلاته' — her prayer invalidates, not his.
2
Trace the original case (asl)
The original case is actual intercourse during prayer. It invalidates both spouses' prayers. Identify the ruling and the reasoning.
Actual intercourse is a major action that invalidates prayer by consensus.
3
Extract the effective cause (illa)
The 'illa is 'being in the meaning of intercourse' (fi ma'na al-jima'). The passage applies qiyas, linking the new case to the asl via this illa.
The illa is not just 'sexual activity' but specifically actions that are precursors to intercourse.
4
Apply the qiyas with the sub-illa of gender
The passage adds a further distinction: because intercourse is the husband's act, his precursors are like the act itself. Also, women have stronger desire, so his touch necessarily arouses her. Therefore, his action invalidates her prayer even without his desire.
Conversely, since intercourse is not the wife's act, her precursors are not in the same category unless the husband desires.
5
Handle the hypothetical objection
The passage anticipates the question: why the difference when both parties lack desire? The response relies on 'shahwah aghlab' (stronger desire in women) and the attribution of intercourse to the husband.
This shows the Hanafi use of talfiq and careful consideration of gender roles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِن ثَبَتَ به الرَّجعة.
لا يحرم الوطء، (وإن ثبت به الرَّجعةُ)، ولو قبلها المصلي، أو لمسها فسدت صلاته؛ لأنه في معنى الجماع، والجماع عمل كثير، كذا في « التجنيس والمزيد » ، وفي ( البزازية »: جامعها زوجها بين الفخذين فيها فسدت صلاتها، وإن لم ينزل، وكذا إذا قبلها بشهوة، أو بغير شهوة، أو لمسها بشهوة؛
(1)1
لأنه في معنى الجماع، بخلاف ما إذا قبلته ولم يشتهها ، ولو نظر إلى فرج مطلقته فـيـهـا صـار مراجعاً، أو نظر حتى ثبتت حرمة المصاهرة لا تفسد الصلاة في المختار، انتهى.
(۱) فإن قلت ما الفرق بين تقبيلها إياه أو لمسها وهو يصلي بغير شهوة منه وبين تقبيله إياها أو لمسه وهي تصلي بغير شهوة أيضاً حيث تفسد صلاتها لا صلاته قلت الفرق أن الشهوة فيهنّ أبلغ فتقبيله مستلزم لاشتهائها. وأيضاً تقبيله مطلقاً ومسه بشهوة في معنى الجماع يعني والجماع فعل الزوج ففعله الدواعـي كـفـعـلـه حقيقـة الجماع، ولو جامعها ولو بين الفخذ تفسد صلاتها فكذا هذا بخلاف المرأة فإن الجماع ليس فعلها فلا يكون إتيانها بالدواعي في معنى الجماع ما لم يشته الزوج. ط.
And if it is established by it (kissing) the return (to marriage). Intercourse is not prohibited. (Even though it establishes the return), if the praying person kisses her or touches her, his prayer becomes invalid; because it is in the meaning of intercourse, and intercourse is a significant action. Thus in al-Tajnīs wal-Mazīd, and in al-Bazzāziyya: If her husband inserted between her thighs while she was praying, her prayer becomes invalid even if no emission occurs. Similarly if he kissed her with desire, or without desire, or touched her with desire; because it is in the meaning of intercourse. Unlike if she kissed him and he did not desire her. And if he looked at the private part of his divorced wife during her waiting period, he becomes a revocable divorcer, or if he looked such that the prohibition of affinity is established, it does not invalidate the prayer in the chosen view. End. (1) For if you say: what is the difference between her kissing or touching him while he is praying without desire, and his kissing or touching her while she is praying also without desire, where her prayer becomes invalid but not his? I say: The difference is that desire in women is stronger, so his kissing necessarily causes her desire. Also, his kissing absolutely and touching with desire is in the meaning of intercourse, meaning intercourse is the act of the husband, so his doing of the precursors is like his actual act of intercourse. And if he had intercourse with her, even if between the thighs, her prayer becomes invalid, so likewise this. Unlike the woman, for intercourse is not her act, so her doing of the precursors is not in the meaning of intercourse unless the husband desires.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah, a commentary on Nur al-Idah, discusses a specific ruling regarding a woman's prayer and the effect of her husband's actions. The passage type is a ruling_with_proof, providing legal reasoning (qiyas) for the difference in how a husband's touch affects the prayer of the wife versus how a wife's touch affects the husband's prayer. The author establishes that if a man kisses or touches his wife during her prayer, her prayer becomes invalid because such actions are akin to intercourse (fi ma'na al-jima'), which is a significant action that invalidates prayer. This holds true whether he does so with desire or without desire (in the case of kissing) and even if there is no emission. The reasoning is that the woman's desire is stronger, so the husband's action naturally arouses her. In contrast, if a woman kisses or touches her husband while he is praying, his prayer does not become invalid, because intercourse is not an act performed by the woman; she does not initiate it in the same way. The passage also notes that looking at the private part of a divorced wife during her waiting period can re-establish marital relations (raj'a) if done with intent, but does not invalidate prayer in the chosen view. The commentary emphasizes the use of 'illa (effective cause) based on the concept of being 'in the meaning of intercourse' and the differential principle (talfiq) based on gender roles.

Key Terms
في معنى الجماع (fi ma'na al-jima'): A legal analogy meaning that an action has the same effective cause ('illa) as intercourse, thus carries the same ruling.
شهوة (shahwah): Sexual desire; a determining factor in whether touch/kiss invalidates prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His prayer becomes invalid.
B) His prayer remains valid, but he should repeat it.
C) His prayer remains valid and does not need repetition.
D) His prayer becomes invalid only if he desires her.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school differentiates between spousal touches during prayer based on gender and the attribution of intercourse, holding that only the husband's action (as the one to whom intercourse is attributed) invalidates the wife's prayer, while the wife's action does not invalidate the husband's prayer unless he experiences desire.

Types of Karahah and Ruling on Repetition of Prayer
Defines makruh, distinguishes tanzih and tahrim, and rules on repetition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi based on evidence or omitted act, and state the ruling on repeating a prayer performed with karahah.

Core Idea
Prayer with any karahah is valid but must be repeated; karahah type depends on wajib or sunnah omission.

In the Hanafi school, not every disliked act in prayer invalidates it. The chapter on makruhat begins with a distinction between two levels of karahah: tanzih (light dislike) and tahrim (prohibitive dislike). This lesson clarifies that distinction and the important ruling that a prayer performed with any karahah is valid (sahih) but should be repeated in a non-disliked manner. Understanding this prevents unnecessary invalidation or neglect of proper precaution.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of makruh
Identify makruh tanzihi (leaving a sunnah) and makruh tahrimi (leaving a wajib). Note: tahrimi is closer to haram, tanzihi closer to halal.
The classification also depends on evidence: zanni prohibition gives tahrim.
2
Determine the basis for classification
Check if the evidence is a zanni prohibition (tahrim) or mere avoidance (tanzih). Alternatively, identify the omitted act: wajib → tahrim, sunnah → tanzih.
These two methods are mentioned as alternative criteria in the text.
3
Understand validity despite karahah
The prayer remains valid because its conditions and pillars are fulfilled. Karahah does not invalidate the act.
This is a key Hanafi principle: sahih but makruh.
4
Learn the ruling on repetition (i'adah)
Every prayer performed with karahah should be repeated in a non-disliked manner. Repetition is wajib for tahrim (omitted wajib) and mandub for tanzih (omitted sunnah).
The source cites al-Tajnīs wal-Mazīd and al-Bazdawi.
5
Address the hadith about prohibition of repetition
The hadith 'No prayer similar to it should be prayed after it' is interpreted as prohibiting repetition due to waswasa, not due to karahah. Thus, it does not conflict.
This interpretation comes from Sadr al-Islam al-Bazdawi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مكروهات الصلاة
يُكْرَهُ لِلْمُصَلِّي سَبْعَةٌ وَسَبْعُونَ شيئاً: تَرْكُ واجب، أو سنة، عمداً.
فصل في مكروهات الصلاة
(۲)
المكروه: ضد المحبوب وحده ما يكون تركه أولى من فعله وتحصيله، كذا قيل، وهذا ظاهر في المكروه تنزيهاً، ويسمى مكروهاً باعتبار المجاور، كالنهي عن الصلاة في أرض الغير، والمكروه في هذا الباب نوعان: أحدهما: ما كره تنزيهاً، والثاني: ما كره تحريماً، فإذا ذكر المكروه لابـد مـن النظر في دليله، فإن كان نهياً ظنياً ) يحكم بكراهة التحريم، إلا لصارف للنهي عن التحريم إلى الندب، وإن لم يكن الدليل نهياً بل كان مفيداً للترك الغير الجازم، فهي تنزيهية)، وقيل: إن تضمن ترك واجب فهو مكروه تحريماً، وإن تضمن ترك سنة فهو مكروه تنزيهاً، انتهى. والمكروه تحريمـاً إلى الحرمة أقرب، والمكروه تنزيهاً إلى الحل أقرب. فالصلاة صحيحة في جميــع صـور الـكـراهـة لاستجماع شرائطها، وتعاد على وجه غير مكروه، وهو الحكم في كل صلاة أديت مع الكراهة. قال في « التجنيس والمزيد » : وكل صلاة أديت مع الكراهة فإنها تعاد لا على وجه الكراهة. وقوله عليـه الصلاة والسلام : « لا يُصَلَّى بَعْدَها صَلاةٌ مثلُها » ) تأويله: النهي عن الإعادة بسبب الوسوسة، فلا
(٤)
يتناول الإعادة بسبب الكراهية، ذكره صدر الإسلام البزدوي في ( الجامع الصغير»، انتهى. وهذا شامل للإعادة بكراهة التنزيه، ولا يمنع منه تمثيل الشيخ أكمل الدين بالواجب في قوله وتعاد على وجه غير مكروه أي تعاد الصلاة للاحتياط على وجه ليس فيه كراهة، وهو الحكم في كل صلاة أديت مع الكراهة، كما إذا ترك واجباً من واجبات الصلاة، انتهى. لأن الإعادة بترك الواجب واجبة، فلا تمنع أن تكون الإعادة مندوبة بترك سنة؛ لأن المكروه موجود بترك السنة، والنكرة في سياق النفي -بقوله: تعاد على وجه ليس فيه كراهة تعم المكروه تنزيهاً وتحريماً.
